In R there are two routes depending on who will use it and how: static (Quarto Dashboard, an HTML you render and share) or interactive (Shiny, a live app where the user filters and explores, which you'll see in Level 3).

The mental model: report vs application

The question that decides everything is simple: does the user need to interact, or just look? If your boss opens a report on Monday with the week's numbers and touches nothing, that's a report: a static Quarto dashboard. If instead they need to pick a region from a menu, drag a date slider and watch everything recalculate live, that's an application: Shiny.

Rule of thumb: static = a "photo" of the data at render time; interactive = a "live query" that responds to every click. The static one is a file; the interactive one is a running server.

Quarto Dashboard (static, fast)

A Quarto dashboard is a normal .qmd file with format: dashboard. The structure is controlled with headings: each ## opens a row (or column), and inside you put valueboxes (those big boxes with a key metric) and charts. You render it once and get a self-contained HTML you email or upload to the web.

Quarto · dashboard.qmd
---
title: "Sales 2026"
format: dashboard
---

## Row {height=20%}

```{r}
#| content: valuebox
#| title: "Total sales"
#| icon: currency-dollar
#| color: primary
list(value = "$1.2M")
```

```{r}
#| content: valuebox
#| title: "Orders"
#| color: success
list(value = "8,430")
```

## Row {height=80%}

```{r}
#| title: "Monthly trend"
library(ggplot2)
ggplot(sales, aes(month, total)) +
  geom_line(linewidth = 1.2) +
  theme_minimal()
```

Read it like a blueprint: the first row (20% of the height) carries two valueboxes side by side —the figures you want to jump out—; the second row (80%) carries the big chart. Quarto distributes the space automatically; you only decide what goes in each row.

The valuebox is the first thing read. Reserve those boxes for the 2 or 4 metrics that truly matter (revenue, conversion, error). A dashboard with twelve valueboxes communicates nothing: when everything is important, nothing is.

Interactive charts: the best part of static

Even though the document is static (there's no server behind it), the charts inside can have client-side interactivity: tooltips on hover, zoom, tables with search. All of that travels packaged in the HTML. Two tools do 90% of the work:

R · plotly + DT
library(plotly)
g <- ggplot(mtcars, aes(wt, mpg, color = factor(cyl))) +
  geom_point(size = 3)
ggplotly(g)      # turns ANY ggplot into interactive (hover, zoom)

library(DT)
datatable(mtcars,               # table with search, sorting and pagination
  filter = "top",
  options = list(pageLength = 5))

The why matters: ggplotly() takes a ggplot you already know how to make and adds tooltips without rewriting anything —hover over a point and you see its exact values, something impossible in a fixed image—. And DT::datatable() turns a data frame into a table where the reader searches, sorts and paginates on their own. With those two, a "static" dashboard feels alive without needing Shiny.

Client-side vs server-side interactivity: plotly and DT run in the reader's browser (embedded JavaScript). They don't need R running behind them, which is why they work in an HTML you send by email. Shiny, on the other hand, needs a server with live R processing every click.

When to use each?

Quarto Dashboard (static)Shiny (interactive)
IdeaAn HTML reportA web application
Ideal forPeriodic metrics, recurring reportsLetting the user filter and explore live
DeploymentA single file: email, Quarto Pub, GitHub PagesNeeds a server (shinyapps.io, Posit Connect)
Cost/maintenanceAlmost noneServer always on

Don't over-engineer: a lot of people jump to Shiny when a Quarto Dashboard would have been enough. If nobody is going to move a filter live, a static HTML is cheaper, faster to load and never goes down. Start static; move up to Shiny only when the interaction is real.

Practice exercise

For each case, decide whether a Quarto Dashboard or Shiny is the better fit, and why: (a) a KPI report emailed every Monday at 8 a.m.; (b) a tool where a manager picks a region and date range and watches the sales recalculate; (c) the results summary of a scientific paper published on the web.

View solution

(a) Quarto Dashboard. It's a recurring report with no interaction: you schedule its rendering (with a cron job or GitHub Actions) and the HTML comes out on its own every Monday. Shiny would be a server left on all week for nothing.

(b) Shiny. There are live inputs (region menu, date slider) that recalculate the output on every change. That's exactly what Shiny does and a static document can't.

(c) Quarto Dashboard. A published paper needs to be a permanent, citable file that doesn't depend on a server that will someday be shut off. If you want some interaction (hover on the figures), ggplotly and DT provide it without leaving the static world.

The underlying logic is always the same: is there user input that should recalculate live? Yes → Shiny. No → Quarto.

Intermediate Level Project
Home/Intermediate Level/Project

★ Capstone project: Dashboard with real data

You're going to build a mini flight-analysis dashboard with nycflights13 (every flight that departed New York in 2013, over 336,000). It's not just writing code: at each step you'll put on the analyst's hat and read what the numbers are telling you.

1 Concept2 Example3 Practice4 Challenge5 Project

Step 0 — The business question

Every analysis starts with a question, not with code. Imagine you work for a New York travel agency and your customers are complaining about delays. Your boss asks you: "Tell me which airlines and which months to recommend flying to arrive on time". That sentence defines what has to be measured: delay by airline and delay by month. Everything that follows is answering that with data.

The dataset: flights has one row per flight. We care about arr_delay (arrival delay in minutes, negative = early), dep_delay (departure delay), carrier (airline code, like "UA") and month. The airlines table translates the code into a readable name.

Step 1 — Prepare the data with the tidyverse

We compute the mean arrival delay per airline. Note two analyst decisions: we filter out the NAs (cancelled flights with no delay value, which we shouldn't average) and we keep n_flights to know whether an average rests on many flights or on a handful.

R · preparation
library(tidyverse)
library(nycflights13)    # install.packages("nycflights13")

delays <- flights |>
  filter(!is.na(arr_delay)) |>              # drop flights with no value
  group_by(carrier) |>
  summarise(
    mean_delay = mean(arr_delay),
    n_flights  = n()
  ) |>
  left_join(airlines, by = "carrier") |>    # code -> readable name
  arrange(desc(mean_delay))

delays
# A tibble: 16 x 4 carrier mean_delay n_flights name <chr> <dbl> <int> <chr> 1 F9 21.9 681 Frontier Airlines Inc. 2 FL 20.1 3175 AirTran Airways Corpor~ 3 EV 15.8 51108 ExpressJet Airlines Inc. 4 YV 15.6 544 Mesa Airlines Inc. 5 OO 11.9 26 SkyWest Airlines Inc. 6 MQ 10.8 25710 Envoy Air # ... 10 more rows

Reading: Frontier (F9) leads with almost 22 minutes of mean delay, but watch out: only 681 flights. ExpressJet (EV), by contrast, arrives 15.8 minutes late across 51,108 flights: that average is rock-solid (in the good sense) and affects far more people. The left_join with airlines was key: without it you'd be staring at codes like "EV" that mean nothing to your boss.

Always look at the sample size: an average computed over 26 flights (SkyWest) isn't comparable to one over 51,000. Before drawing conclusions, check n_flights. That reflex is what separates an analyst from someone who just sorts columns.

Step 2 — The top-10 chart by airline

With the data ready, we visualize the 10 worst. We use coord_flip() so the airline names read horizontally (never make the reader of your dashboard tilt their head) and reorder() so the bars come out sorted by magnitude.

R · bar chart
delays |>
  slice_max(mean_delay, n = 10) |>
  ggplot(aes(x = reorder(name, mean_delay), y = mean_delay)) +
  geom_col(fill = "#2563EB") +
  coord_flip() +
  labs(title = "Top 10 airlines by mean delay (NYC, 2013)",
       x = NULL, y = "Mean arrival delay (min)") +
  theme_minimal(base_size = 13)

Reading: the chart visually orders the problem. The longest bar is the airline to avoid; the ones at the bottom, hovering around zero or negative, are the ones that tend to arrive on time or early. This is the chart that directly answers the first half of the boss's question: "which airlines?".

Step 3 — The temporal pattern by month

Now the second half: in which months is it worse? A line chart is ideal for seeing seasonality —the eye follows the rise and fall across the year—.

R · time series
flights |>
  group_by(month) |>
  summarise(delay = mean(dep_delay, na.rm = TRUE)) |>
  ggplot(aes(month, delay)) +
  geom_line(color = "#06B6D4", linewidth = 1.2) +
  geom_point(color = "#06B6D4", size = 2) +
  scale_x_continuous(breaks = 1:12) +
  labs(title = "Mean departure delay by month (NYC, 2013)",
       x = "Month", y = "Mean departure delay (min)") +
  theme_minimal(base_size = 13)

Reading: two clear peaks appear: June-July (summer storms and peak travel season) and December (snow and year-end holidays). The calmest months are September-November. Translation for the business: if your customer can choose, have them fly in autumn; if they travel in summer or at Christmas, have them accept the risk and buy connection insurance.

Each chart answers one question. Bars → "who?"; line → "when?". A good dashboard isn't a gallery of pretty charts: it's a set where each figure answers something someone cares about.

Step 4 — Suggested steps to enrich it

With the above you already have a solid dashboard. These three pieces raise it to something that impresses in an interview. I'll give you the code and what to look for in each one.

a) Most frequent destination airports

R · top destinations
flights |>
  count(dest, sort = TRUE) |>
  left_join(airports, by = c("dest" = "faa")) |>   # code -> real name
  slice_max(n, n = 5) |>
  select(name = name, flights = n)
# A tibble: 5 x 2 name flights <chr> <int> 1 Chicago Ohare Intl 17283 2 Hartsfield Jackson Atlanta Intl 17215 3 Los Angeles Intl 16174 4 Gen Edward Lawrence Logan Intl 15508 5 Orlando Intl 14082

What to read: the destinations that matter most to operations. Chicago and Atlanta (two big hubs) dominate. A value box with "destination #1" tells the story of where New York really flies.

b) Distance-delay relationship

R · scatter
flights |>
  filter(!is.na(arr_delay)) |>
  group_by(dest) |>
  summarise(
    mean_dist  = mean(distance),
    mean_delay = mean(arr_delay),
    n          = n()
  ) |>
  ggplot(aes(mean_dist, mean_delay, size = n)) +
  geom_point(alpha = 0.5, color = "#2563EB") +
  labs(title = "Do long flights arrive later?",
       x = "Mean distance (miles)", y = "Mean delay (min)")

What to read: is there a trend? In general, longer flights make up delay in the air (they arrive less late than they departed), so you'll see the cloud not rise with distance. The point size (size = n) keeps a destination with two flights from weighing as much as one with thousands.

c) On-time percentage

R · punctuality KPI
flights |>
  filter(!is.na(arr_delay)) |>
  summarise(
    pct_ontime = mean(arr_delay <= 0) * 100    # % arriving on time or early
  )
# A tibble: 1 x 1 pct_ontime <dbl> 1 58.5

What to read: this single number, 58.5%, is a perfect value box. The trick is mean(arr_delay <= 0): when you average a vector of TRUE/FALSE, R counts the proportion of TRUE. It's the idiomatic shortcut for "what percentage meets a condition". Translation: almost 4 out of 10 flights arrive late. An honest KPI your boss understands without explanation.

Step 5 — Assemble it in a Quarto Dashboard

The final step turns your loose analyses into a product. Paste each block into a .qmd with format: dashboard: the single-figure KPIs go as valueboxes at the top, and the charts below, spread across rows.

Quarto · flights.qmd
---
title: "NYC Flights 2013"
format: dashboard
---

## Row {height=25%}

```{r}
#| content: valuebox
#| title: "Overall punctuality"
#| color: success
list(value = "58.5%")
```

```{r}
#| content: valuebox
#| title: "Worst airline"
#| color: danger
list(value = "Frontier")
```

## Row {height=75%}

```{r}
#| title: "Top 10 airlines by delay"
# ...the ggplot from Step 2 goes here...
```

```{r}
#| title: "Delay by month"
# ...the ggplot from Step 3 goes here...
```

One quarto render flights.qmd and you have a self-contained HTML that answers your boss's original question at a single glance. That's a deliverable, not a script.

Put all of this in a Quarto document with format: dashboard and you'll have a professional dashboard renderable to HTML, ready to share. Wrap each chart with ggplotly() and your figures will gain tooltips with no extra work.

Challenge

Extend the dashboard and publish it. (a) The 5 most frequent destination airports — hint: count(dest, sort = TRUE) + left_join(airports, by = c("dest" = "faa")). (b) Distance vs delay — hint: group by dest, summarize mean distance and delay, and use geom_point(aes(size = n)). (c) Value box with the on-time % — hint: mean(arr_delay <= 0) * 100. As a bonus challenge, add a value box with your most reliable airline (highest on-time % and at least 5,000 flights) and publish it on Quarto Pub or GitHub Pages.

Intermediate Level complete! You no longer just write code: you prepare data, interrogate it like an analyst, turn it into charts that answer questions and assemble it into a dashboard someone can use to decide. You command the tidyverse, visualization, reporting, SQL and dashboards. In the Advanced Level you enter professional territory: functional programming, Machine Learning, Shiny and deployment. See you upstairs.

Advanced Level
Home/Advanced Level/Functional programming

Functional programming

R is, at its core, a functional language: functions are first-class objects, passed as arguments, returned as results, and stored in lists. The purrr package pushes that paradigm to its fullest with a family of functions that is coherent, predictable, and type-safe.

By the end you'll know: how to replace loops with the map family, choosing the right suffix for the type of output; how to combine several vectors with map2/pmap; how to accumulate results with reduce/accumulate; and how to harden your pipelines against errors with safely, possibly, and quietly.

Mental model: apply a function to each element

A for loop mixes three things: what you iterate over, what you do at each step, and where you store the result. Functional programming separates those responsibilities. The core idea is simple: "take this collection and apply this function to each element". You describe the transformation; purrr takes care of iterating, accumulating, and returning a predictable structure. The result is shorter code, with no index variables, no accumulators to initialize, and none of that class of off-by-one index errors (i+1) that plague loops.

The map family and type safety

Every map_*() function takes the same inputs (a collection and a function) and differs only in the type of output it guarantees. That suffix is not cosmetic: it's a contract. If map_dbl() hits an element it can't coerce to double, it fails immediately with a clear error instead of silently returning a list full of garbage that will blow up three steps later. Programming with explicit types is defensive programming.

R · purrr
library(purrr)

map(1:3, \(x) x^2)              # ALWAYS returns a list: list(1, 4, 9)
map_dbl(1:3, \(x) x^2)          # double vector:  1 4 9
map_chr(letters[1:3], toupper) # character vector: "A" "B" "C"
map_lgl(1:5, \(x) x %% 2 == 0) # logical vector: FALSE TRUE FALSE TRUE FALSE
map_int(1:3, \(x) x * 2L)      # integer vector: 2 4 6

# The \(x) syntax is R's native anonymous function (>= 4.1),
# equivalent to function(x) but more compact. It replaces purrr's old ~ .x.
List of 3 $ : num 1 $ : num 4 $ : num 9 [1] 1 4 9 [1] "A" "B" "C" [1] FALSE TRUE FALSE TRUE FALSE [1] 2 4 6

Common mistake: reaching for map() "just in case" and then wrestling with the resulting list (unlist(), [[1]], etc.). If you expect numbers, ask for map_dbl(). If an element breaks the type, you want to find out right there, not drag an unexpected list all the way to the end of the pipeline.

Multiple arguments: map2, pmap, and imap

When the function needs more than one input vector, you level up. map2() walks two collections in parallel; pmap() walks a list of N arguments (ideal for iterating over the rows of a data frame); imap() also hands you the index or the name of each element.

R · purrr
# map2: two inputs in parallel
prices   <- c(100, 250, 80)
discount <- c(0.10, 0.20, 0.05)
map2_dbl(prices, discount, \(p, d) p * (1 - d))   # 90 200 76

# pmap: iterate over the rows of a data frame (each column = one argument)
orders <- tibble::tibble(
  product  = c("Keyboard", "Monitor", "Mouse"),
  quantity = c(2, 1, 3),
  price    = c(120, 900, 60)
)
pmap_dbl(orders, \(product, quantity, price, ...) quantity * price)  # 240 900 180

# imap: value + name/index at the same time
sales <- c(north = 1200, south = 800, center = 1500)
imap_chr(sales, \(amount, region) paste0(region, ": $", amount))
[1] 90 200 76 [1] 240 900 180 north south center "north: $1200" "south: $800" "center: $1500"

In pmap, add ... at the end of your anonymous function so it ignores columns you don't use. That way the same pmap keeps working even if the data frame has extra columns.

walk: iterate for the side effect

Sometimes you don't want a value back: you want to do something — save files, print, write to a database. That's what walk() is for. It does exactly what map() does but discards the result and returns the input invisibly, so it slots into a pipe without cluttering the console with a list of NULLs.

R · purrr
tables <- list(customers = df_customers, sales = df_sales)
paths  <- paste0("output/", names(tables), ".csv")

# walk2: save each table to its path, returning nothing
walk2(tables, paths, \(data, path) readr::write_csv(data, path))

# iwalk: use the element's name as part of the effect
iwalk(tables, \(data, name) message("Saved table '", name, "' with ", nrow(data), " rows"))

reduce and accumulate

reduce() collapses a collection to a single value by applying a two-argument function cumulatively: f(f(f(x1, x2), x3), x4). accumulate() is its sibling that keeps every intermediate step — perfect for running balances or cumulative sums.

R · purrr
reduce(1:5, `+`)                      # 15  (collapses to a single value)
accumulate(1:5, `+`)                  # 1 3 6 10 15  (running total)

# Real case: join N tables on a common key in a single line
tables <- list(df_sales, df_customers, df_regions)
reduce(tables, \(a, b) dplyr::left_join(a, b, by = "id"))

# reduce with an initial value (.init) to seed the accumulator
reduce(list(c(1,2), c(2,3), c(3,4)), union, .init = integer(0))  # 1 2 3 4
[1] 15 [1] 1 3 6 10 15 [1] 1 2 3 4

Robust pipelines: safely, possibly, and quietly

In the real world, one of your 500 files is corrupt, one of your 200 API calls fails, a model doesn't converge. A single error halts the whole map and you lose the work of the other 499. purrr's adverbs turn a fragile function into a fault-tolerant one:

  • safely(f) returns a list with $result and $error (one of them is NULL). It never fails.
  • possibly(f, otherwise) returns a default value when there's an error. Simpler if you don't care about the message.
  • quietly(f) captures the warnings and printed output, in addition to the result.
R · purrr
safe_log <- safely(log)

results <- map(list(1, 10, "a", -1), safe_log)
# Each element is list(result = ..., error = ...)

# Split into two clean lists: the ones that worked and the ones that failed
transposed <- purrr::transpose(results)
successes <- purrr::compact(transposed$result)   # values that were computed
errors    <- purrr::compact(transposed$error)    # captured error objects

length(successes)   # 3  (log(1), log(10), log(-1)=NaN with warning but no error)
length(errors)      # 1  (the string "a" does throw an error)

# possibly: more direct when you just want a default
log_or_na <- possibly(log, otherwise = NA_real_)
map_dbl(list(1, 10, "a"), log_or_na)   # 0.000 2.303 NA
[1] 3 [1] 1 [1] 0.000000 2.302585 NA

Best practice: in any map that touches the outside world (files, network, databases), wrap the function with safely() or possibly(). Process everything that can be processed first and review the failures at the end; don't let a single element take down the whole batch.

Real case 1: read and combine several CSVs from a folder

The most common pattern in analytics: you have a folder with one file per month (or per branch) and you want a single table. With map it's three lines, and with set_names + .id you keep track of which file each row came from.

R · purrr + readr
library(purrr)
library(readr)

files <- list.files("data/sales", pattern = "\\.csv$", full.names = TRUE)

sales <- files |>
  set_names(basename) |>                       # name each element by its file
  map(\(path) read_csv(path, show_col_types = FALSE)) |>
  list_rbind(names_to = "source_file")         # stack and add a provenance column

# In a folder with 2024-01.csv ... 2024-12.csv:
dim(sales)                                     # e.g. 48000 rows, columns + 1
dplyr::count(sales, source_file)               # how many rows each month contributed
# A tibble: 12 × 2 source_file n <chr> <int> 1 2024-01.csv 4102 2 2024-02.csv 3980 3 2024-03.csv 4210 # ... 9 more rows

list_rbind() and list_cbind() (purrr 1.0+) replace the old map_df()/map_dfr(), which are now "superseded". They do the same thing but are more explicit: first map() to a list, then combine.

Real case 2: one model per group

The split → map(model) → map(coefficients) pattern is the crown jewel of functional programming: you train an independent model for each group and extract what interests you, all in a readable pipe with not a single loop.

R · purrr
# How does fuel use (mpg) change with weight (wt), by number of cylinders?
models <- mtcars |>
  split(mtcars$cyl) |>                     # a list: one data frame per cylinder count
  map(\(df) lm(mpg ~ wt, data = df))        # one model per group

# Coefficients of each model
map(models, coef)

# R² of each model, as a named numeric vector
map_dbl(models, \(m) summary(m)$r.squared)
$`4` (Intercept) wt 39.571196 -5.647025 $`6` (Intercept) wt 28.408845 -2.780106 $`8` (Intercept) wt 23.868090 -2.192438 4 6 8 0.5086326 0.4645102 0.4229655

purrr vs. the base apply family

Base R already ships lapply, sapply, vapply, and mapply, and they work fine. So why purrr? Consistency. In base, sapply() sometimes returns a vector, sometimes a matrix, and sometimes a list depending on the data — unpredictable in production. In purrr, the suffix in the name always tells you what you'll get. The argument order is uniform (data first, function second, ideal for the pipe), and the adverbs safely/possibly have no direct equivalent.

R · equivalences
# base            ->  purrr
lapply(x, f)      #    map(x, f)
sapply(x, f)      #    map_dbl(x, f) / map_chr(x, f)   (explicit type!)
vapply(x, f, num) #    map_dbl(x, f)
mapply(f, x, y)   #    map2(x, y, f)
Map(f, x, y)      #    map2(x, y, f)

Of the base family, the safest is vapply(): it forces you to declare the output type and length, just like purrr's suffixes. If you'd rather avoid an extra dependency, vapply() is your ally.

Guided exercise

You have a folder data/branches with one CSV per branch, each with columns date, amount, and category. Build a pipeline that: (1) reads and combines all the files, adding a column with the branch name; (2) is robust to a corrupt file (one bad file shouldn't blow up the whole batch); and (3) returns the total amount per branch. Use purrr.

View solution

Step 1 — list and name the files. We use set_names(basename) so each element carries its file's name, which later becomes the provenance column.

Step 2 — robust reading. We wrap read_csv with safely(). That way a bad file produces a captured error instead of halting the map.

Step 3 — separate successes from failures with transpose + compact, stack only the good ones, and aggregate.

R · solution
library(purrr)
library(readr)
library(dplyr)

safe_read <- safely(\(path) read_csv(path, show_col_types = FALSE))

raw <- list.files("data/branches", pattern = "\\.csv$", full.names = TRUE) |>
  set_names(basename) |>
  map(safe_read)

# Warn about the ones that failed and keep the good ones
parts  <- transpose(raw)
failed <- compact(parts$error)
if (length(failed) > 0)
  walk(names(failed), \(f) warning("File skipped: ", f))

sales <- parts$result |>
  compact() |>                                  # drop the NULLs from the failures
  list_rbind(names_to = "branch")

# Total amount per branch
sales |>
  group_by(branch) |>
  summarise(total = sum(amount, na.rm = TRUE)) |>
  arrange(desc(total))

Keys: safely isolates the failures, compact cleans out the NULLs, list_rbind(names_to=) stacks and labels in one step, and the final group_by |> summarise aggregates. Zero loops, fault-tolerant, and readable top to bottom.

Challenge

With the gapminder dataset (gapminder package), fit a linear model of life expectancy against year (lifeExp ~ year) per country, extract the slope of each model, and build an ordered table showing the 5 countries where life expectancy grew fastest and the 5 where it grew slowest (or declined). Solve it with the split → map → map_dbl pattern and without using for loops.

In short: (1) the suffixes map_dbl/chr/lgl/int/df turn the output type into a contract that fails early and clearly; (2) map2/pmap/imap scale to several arguments and walk covers side effects; (3) reduce/accumulate collapse collections; (4) safely/possibly/quietly make your pipelines fault-tolerant. The star pattern: split → map(model) → map(coef).

Go deeper: purrr.tidyverse.org · R for Data Science: Iteration · Advanced R: Functionals.

Advanced Level
Home/Advanced Level/Optimization

Code optimization

Before you optimize, measure. "Premature optimization is the root of all evil" (Donald Knuth). The instinct that "this is surely slow" is right less often than you'd think: the real bottleneck is almost never where you imagine it. Identify it with data, and only then act.

By the end you'll know: how to measure time and memory with system.time, bench::mark, and profvis; how to read a benchmark table; and how to apply optimization strategies in order of impact (vectorize, pre-allocate, efficient structures, parallelize, and, as a last resort, C++).

Golden rule: measure, don't guess

Optimizing without measuring is like operating without a diagnosis. There are three levels of measurement, from coarsest to most detailed:

  • system.time() — a quick stopwatch for one expression. Good for a single-pass "how long does this take?".
  • bench::mark() — a serious benchmark: it runs each expression many times, reports the median, percentiles, memory allocations, and even verifies that all the alternatives return the same result.
  • profvis::profvis() — a visual profiler: it tells you which specific line eats up the time inside a large function.

Measuring with bench::mark

Let's compare three ways to sum the same range: a loop that grows an accumulator, a vectorized version, and one with purrr. bench::mark checks that all three return the same thing and orders them by speed.

R · bench
library(bench)

n <- 1e5
res <- mark(
  loop       = { s <- 0; for (i in 1:n) s <- s + i; s },
  vectorized = sum(as.numeric(1:n)),
  purrr_red  = purrr::reduce(1:n, `+`),
  iterations = 50
)
res[, c("expression", "median", "mem_alloc", "n_gc")]
# A tibble: 3 × 4 expression median mem_alloc n_gc <bch:expr> <bch:tm> <bch:byt> <dbl> 1 loop 3.9ms 896B 0 2 vectorized 78us 784KB 0 3 purrr_red 16ms 1.6MB 12 # The vectorized version is ~50x faster than the loop; # reduce is convenient but slow for simple arithmetic operations.

How to read the table: median is the typical time (more robust than the mean against system spikes); mem_alloc is how much memory was requested (a good predictor of slowness: more allocations = more work for the garbage collector); n_gc counts how many times the garbage collector ran during the iterations. Many gcs are a sign you're creating too many copies.

Strategies from highest to lowest impact

Optimize in this order: start with what gives the most gain for the least risk, and go lower only if you need to.

  1. Vectorize: replace loops with operations over whole vectors and map/apply functions. R is written in C underneath; a vectorized operation runs in C, whereas an R loop runs in the interpreter.
  2. Pre-allocate memory: never grow a vector with c(v, x) or v[[length(v)+1]] <- x inside a loop. Each growth copies the entire vector. Create the container at its final size and fill it in.
  3. Use efficient structures: data.table or collapse for aggregations over millions of rows; matrices instead of data frames for pure numeric computation.
  4. Parallelize: spread the work across cores with furrr::future_map() over a future plan. It only pays off when each task is substantial (the dispatch has a fixed cost).
  5. Rewrite the critical part in C++ with Rcpp. It's the last resort: maximum performance, maximum maintenance complexity. Reserve it for the 1% of code that truly warrants it.

Pre-allocation: the classic mistake

R · pattern
# BAD: the vector grows every pass -> R copies everything over and over (O(n^2))
result <- c()
for (i in 1:n) result <- c(result, i^2)

# GOOD: reserve the final size and fill in by index (O(n))
result <- numeric(n)
for (i in 1:n) result[i] <- i^2

# BEST: vectorized, no loop
result <- (1:n)^2

Common mistake: "R is slow with loops". That's not quite true: R is slow when you grow objects inside loops. A loop over a pre-allocated vector is perfectly acceptable. The silent killer is the repeated copy caused by reassignment.

Parallelizing with furrr

If you have independent, heavy tasks (fitting 500 models, processing 500 images), furrr gives you the same purrr API but spread across cores. You swap map for future_map and declare a plan.

R · furrr + future
library(furrr)
plan(multisession, workers = 4)         # use 4 processes in parallel

# future_map is identical to map, but distributes the work
results <- future_map(1:500, \(i) expensive_task(i))

plan(sequential)                        # go back to normal mode when done

80% of the gains come from 20% of the code. Don't optimize functions that run once; focus on what runs millions of times. And remember: parallelizing has overhead — for trivial tasks, four cores can be slower than one because of the cost of spinning up processes and moving data.

Memory: measure and free

R
x <- rnorm(1e6)
object.size(x)                 # size of an object
format(object.size(x), units = "MB")   # "7.6 Mb"

lobstr::obj_size(x)            # modern variant, accounts for shared memory

rm(x)                          # remove the reference
gc()                           # suggest the collector free memory
8000048 bytes [1] "7.6 Mb"

Best practice: always measure before and after optimizing, and record the number. "It feels faster" is not evidence. A reproducible benchmark with bench::mark is. And don't sacrifice readability for a 5% speedup in code that isn't a bottleneck.

Guided exercise

You need to compute a 3-period moving average over a vector of 100,000 values. Write two versions —one with a pre-allocated loop and one vectorized— and compare them with bench::mark. Verify they give the same result and report how many times faster the vectorized version is.

View solution

Step 1 — data and loop version. We pre-allocate the output vector so we don't fall into the O(n²) mistake.

Step 2 — vectorized version. The 3-period moving average is (x[i-1] + x[i] + x[i+1]) / 3; with vector shifts we do it without a loop using head/tail.

Step 3 — benchmark. bench::mark validates by default that both outputs match (if not, it throws an error).

R · solution
library(bench)
set.seed(42)
x <- rnorm(1e5)

# Pre-allocated loop version (centered 3-period moving average)
mm_loop <- function(x) {
  n <- length(x)
  out <- rep(NA_real_, n)
  for (i in 2:(n - 1)) out[i] <- (x[i - 1] + x[i] + x[i + 1]) / 3
  out
}

# Vectorized version with shifts
mm_vec <- function(x) {
  n <- length(x)
  middle <- (head(x, -2) + x[2:(n - 1)] + tail(x, -2)) / 3
  c(NA, middle, NA)
}

res <- mark(
  loop = mm_loop(x),
  vec  = mm_vec(x),
  iterations = 50
)
res[, c("expression", "median", "mem_alloc")]

# How many times faster (median loop / median vec)
as.numeric(res$median[1]) / as.numeric(res$median[2])
# A tibble: 2 × 3 expression median mem_alloc <bch:expr> <bch:tm> <bch:byt> 1 loop 6.2ms 1.53MB 2 vec 310us 3.05MB [1] 20.0 # the vectorized version is ~20x faster

The vectorized version is an order of magnitude faster because all the arithmetic happens in C over whole vectors, while the loop interprets 99,998 iterations in R. Note the trade-off: the vectorized version uses a bit more memory (it creates intermediate vectors), but wins handily on time.

Challenge

Simulate 1,000 experiments where each one rolls 10,000 dice and counts how many sixes come up. Implement it three ways: (a) a nested loop, (b) purrr::map_int with vectorized sampling inside, and (c) furrr::future_map_int in parallel. Use bench::mark to compare them and explain under what conditions the parallel version starts to win (hint: raise the cost per task and watch for the crossover point).

In short: (1) measure before optimizing — system.time for a quick look, bench::mark to compare, profvis to locate the guilty line; (2) the order of impact is vectorize > pre-allocate > efficient structures > parallelize > C++; (3) the real enemy is growing objects in loops, not the loops themselves; (4) parallelizing only pays off with large, independent tasks.

Go deeper: Advanced R: Improving performance · bench (official pkgdown) · furrr (parallelism).

Home/Advanced Level/data.table

data.table

data.table is the go-to tool for big data in R: extremely fast and memory-efficient, with a concise syntax that fits inside a pair of brackets. When dplyr starts to sweat with tens of millions of rows, data.table barely breaks a sweat.

By the end you'll know: how to read the dt[i, j, by] syntax as a sentence; how to modify columns by reference with := and why it saves memory; how to speed things up with setkey; how to use .SD/.SDcols to operate over several columns; how to chain operations; and how to decide between data.table, dplyr, and the dtplyr bridge.

Mental model: dt[i, j, by]

All the power of data.table lives in a single expression with three slots. Read it as a sentence: "take the rows i, compute j, grouping by by". If you know SQL, the mapping is direct: i is the WHERE, j is the SELECT, and by is the GROUP BY. That economy of syntax is what makes the code so compact.

R · data.table
library(data.table)
dt <- as.data.table(mtcars)

dt[mpg > 20]                              # i: filter rows
dt[, .(mean_mpg = mean(mpg))]             # j: compute (.() creates output columns)
dt[, .(mean_mpg = mean(mpg)), by = cyl]   # empty i, j summarizes, by groups
dt[order(-mpg)]                           # i can sort
dt[cyl == 6, .(mean_hp = mean(hp)), by = gear]   # all three slots together
cyl mean_mpg <num> <num> 1: 6 19.74286 2: 4 26.66364 3: 8 15.10000

.() is an alias for list() inside the bracket. You use it in j to name the output columns and in by to group by several columns: by = .(cyl, gear).

:= modifies by reference (and why it matters)

Here is the heart of data.table's efficiency. In base R and dplyr, when you add or change a column, R creates a full copy of the data frame ("copy-on-modify" semantics). With 50 million rows, that copy costs seconds and momentarily doubles the memory. The := operator modifies the table in place, by reference: it copies nothing. That's why data.table can work with datasets that nearly fill your RAM.

R · data.table
dt[, weight_kg := wt * 453.6]             # create a column WITHOUT copying the table

# Several columns at once
dt[, `:=`(
  efficient = mpg > 25,
  hp_per_kg = hp / (wt * 453.6)
)]

# Update only a subset of rows (i + :=)
dt[cyl == 8, category := "high consumption"]

# Delete a column by reference
dt[, weight_kg := NULL]

Careful with modification by reference: since := doesn't copy, if you do dt2 <- dt and then dt2[, x := 1], you also change dt (both names point to the same object). If you need a truly independent copy, use copy(dt) explicitly.

setkey and keys: lightning-fast lookups

Setting a key (setkey) physically sorts the table by that column and lets data.table use binary search instead of scanning row by row. Filtering by a keyed column goes from O(n) to O(log n): the difference between milliseconds and minutes on huge tables.

R · data.table
setkey(dt, cyl)                # sort by cyl and mark the key
key(dt)                        # "cyl"

dt[.(6)]                       # binary search by the key: rows with cyl == 6
dt[.(c(4, 8))]                 # several keys at once

# Ultra-fast key-based joins
setkey(sales, customer_id)
setkey(customers, customer_id)
sales[customers]              # join on the common key

.SD and .SDcols: operating over many columns

.SD ("Subset of Data") is the subset of the data.table within each group, excluding the grouping columns. Combined with lapply it lets you apply the same operation to many columns at once; .SDcols picks which ones.

R · data.table
# Mean of several numeric columns, by group
dt[, lapply(.SD, mean), by = cyl, .SDcols = c("mpg", "hp", "wt")]

# Apply a function to ALL numeric columns
num_cols <- names(dt)[sapply(dt, is.numeric)]
dt[, lapply(.SD, max), .SDcols = num_cols]
cyl mpg hp wt <num> <num> <num> <num> 1: 6 19.74286 122.28571 3.117143 2: 4 26.66364 82.63636 2.285727 3: 8 15.10000 209.21429 3.999214

Chaining and ultra-fast reading

You can chain brackets one after another, like an implicit pipe: dt[...][...][...]. And for reading/writing large files, fread/fwrite are several times faster than their base or readr equivalents, detecting types and separators automatically.

R · data.table
# Chaining: filter -> group -> sort the result
dt[mpg > 15][, .(max_hp = max(hp)), by = gear][order(-max_hp)]

# Fast reading and writing (multi-core by default)
big <- fread("transactions.csv")          # reads millions of rows in seconds
fwrite(big, "output.csv")                 # writes just as fast

Real case: aggregation over transactions

Suppose a file of 10 million transactions with columns date, store, category, and amount. We want total sales and the average ticket per store and category, keeping only the combinations above a certain threshold. All in a single chain, reading with fread and aggregating by reference.

R · data.table
library(data.table)

tx <- fread("transactions.csv")           # 10,000,000 rows in ~2-3 s

summary <- tx[
    amount > 0,                                      # i: drop void transactions
    .(total = sum(amount),                           # j: two metrics
      avg_ticket = mean(amount),
      n_transactions = .N),                          # .N = number of rows in the group
    by = .(store, category)                          # by: double grouping
  ][total > 1e6                                      # chained: filter large groups
  ][order(-total)]                                   # and sort highest to lowest

head(summary, 5)
store category total avg_ticket n_transactions <char> <char> <num> <num> <int> 1: North Beverages 4820150 42.11 114467 2: Center Groceries 3990870 38.55 103522 3: South Beverages 3110240 40.02 77716 4: North Cleaning 2455900 55.31 44401 5: Center Beverages 2201050 39.88 55192

.N is a special shortcut: inside j it equals the number of rows in the current group. dt[, .N, by = store] is the canonical way to count by group, faster than any alternative.

dplyr, data.table, or dtplyr?

It's not a war; it's choosing the right tool for the job:

  • dplyr — more readable and expressive, with English verbs and the pipe. Perfect for interactive analysis and moderately sized data (up to a few million rows). It's what you'll use 90% of the time.
  • data.table — wins when performance and memory are critical: tens or hundreds of millions of rows, processes that run in a loop, servers with limited RAM.
  • dtplyr — the bridge: you write dplyr syntax and it's translated and executed with the data.table engine. It gives you speed without abandoning the grammar you already know.
R · dtplyr
library(dtplyr)
library(dplyr)

lazy <- lazy_dt(tx)             # wrap the data.table (lazy evaluation)

lazy |>
  filter(amount > 0) |>
  group_by(store, category) |>
  summarise(total = sum(amount), .groups = "drop") |>
  arrange(desc(total)) |>
  as_tibble()                   # triggers execution with the data.table engine

Best practice: first learn to read dt[i, j, by] as a sentence before writing complex things. And remember the := trap: it modifies by reference, so use copy() when you want an independent version. For reading large files, fread is almost always the best call, whether you use dplyr or data.table afterward.

Guided exercise

With the data.table dt <- as.data.table(ggplot2::diamonds) (about 54,000 rows): (1) create by reference a column price_per_carat = price / carat; (2) obtain, for each combination of cut and color, the mean price, the mean price per carat, and the number of diamonds; and (3) keep only the groups with more than 500 diamonds, ordered by mean price descending. Do it in a single chain.

View solution

Step 1 — column by reference. We use := so we don't copy the table.

Step 2 — double aggregation. We group by .(cut, color) and compute three metrics in j, including .N for the count.

Step 3 — chain the large-group filter and the ordering.

R · solution
library(data.table)
dt <- as.data.table(ggplot2::diamonds)

# 1) column by reference
dt[, price_per_carat := price / carat]

# 2) and 3) aggregation + chaining
summary <- dt[
    , .(mean_price = mean(price),
        mean_ppc   = mean(price_per_carat),
        n          = .N),
    by = .(cut, color)
  ][n > 500
  ][order(-mean_price)]

head(summary, 5)
cut color mean_price mean_ppc n <ord> <ord> <num> <num> <int> 1: Premium J 6294.592 3921.34 808 2: Fair J 4975.655 2851.98 ... 3: Good J 4574.173 ... ... # (illustrative values; the exact order depends on the data)

Keys to the solution: := adds the column without copying; by = .(cut, color) groups by two variables; .N counts rows per group; and the chain [...][n > 500][order(...)] filters and sorts the aggregated result with no intermediate variables.

Challenge

Generate (or download) a CSV of ~5 million rows of fictional transactions (user_id, date, amount). Read it with fread and compute, per user: total spend, number of purchases, and the number of days between their first and last transaction (recency). Then measure with bench::mark the time of the aggregation in data.table versus the same operation in dplyr over a tibble, and also compare with dtplyr. How much faster is the data.table engine on your machine?

In short: (1) dt[i, j, by] reads as "filter i, compute j, group by" and maps to WHERE/SELECT/GROUP BY; (2) := modifies by reference without copying, hence its memory efficiency —use copy() if you want independence; (3) setkey enables binary search and .SD/.SDcols operate over many columns; (4) choose dplyr for readability, data.table for scale, and dtplyr as the bridge between the two.

Go deeper: data.table (official site) · Introductory vignette · dtplyr.tidyverse.org.

Object-oriented programming

R has several object systems that coexist in the same language. Knowing them lets you understand how packages work under the hood, extend them with your own types, and pick the right tool depending on whether you need something informal and quick or something formal with mutable state.

By the end you'll know: how to tell apart R's four object systems (S3, S4, R6, S7) and when to use each; how to explain S3 generic method dispatch (why summary(model) ends up calling summary.lm); and how to create your own S3 class with a constructor and custom print and summary methods, watching dispatch work in real time.

Why R has four object systems

Unlike Java or Python, where OOP is built around a single idea of a "class", R accumulated several systems over the course of its history. It isn't a mess: each one solves a different problem. The key mental model is that in R most of these systems are functional: objects hold data, and generic functions decide what to do based on the class of the object they receive. This is the opposite of the Java model, where the method "lives inside" the object (object.method()). In R you write method(object) and R looks up the right implementation.

SystemStyleWhen to use it
S3Functional, informal90% of R. Based on class() and methods by naming convention. Ideal for almost everything: lightweight classes, print/summary/plot methods, extending existing objects.
S4Functional, formalWhen you need strict validation, multiple inheritance, and guaranteed contracts. Dominant in Bioconductor and large packages with many contributors.
R6Encapsulated, mutableWhen you need real mutable state: objects that change in place. The basis of modern Shiny (reactiveValues), database connections, state machines.
S7Functional, modern formalReleased in 2024 by the R Consortium to unify S3 and S4: clean syntax, validation, and multiple dispatch. ggplot2 4.0 already adopts it. It's the future of formal OOP in R.

S3 method dispatch: how R "picks" the method

This is the most important concept at this level. When you write summary(model), there is no single giant summary function full of if statements. There is a tiny generic function whose only job is to look at the object's class and forward the call to the right method. The mental model is literal: R reads the object's class label and looks for a function named generic.class.

R · the generic under the hood
# An S3 generic is just this: a function that calls UseMethod()
summary
#> function (object, ...)
#> UseMethod("summary")

# Fit a linear model
model <- lm(dist ~ speed, data = cars)
class(model)
#> [1] "lm"

# When you write summary(model), R:
# 1) reads class(model) -> "lm"
# 2) looks for a function named summary.lm  -> finds it
# 3) calls summary.lm(model)
summary(model)      # dispatches to summary.lm automatically

If the class were a vector of several labels, R tries them one by one in order until it finds a method. If it finds none, it falls back to generic.default. That's why almost everything in R "just works": print, summary, plot, [, +, and hundreds more are generics that dispatch by class.

R · watching dispatch
x <- factor(c("a", "b", "a"))
class(x)
#> [1] "factor"

# print(x) dispatches to print.factor; if it didn't exist, to print.default
print(x)
#> [1] a b a
#> Levels: a b

# You can list ALL the methods of a generic
methods(summary) |> head(4)
#> [1] summary.aov     summary.data.frame  summary.Date    summary.factor

# And check which class a specific method belongs to
existsMethod  # (for S4) — in S3 it's enough to: exists("summary.lm")

Real example: an S3 bank "account" class

Let's build a complete class with the three pieces every well-made S3 object should have: a constructor (creates and labels the object), a print method (how it's displayed), and a summary method (a computed summary). You'll see dispatch working without ever calling print.account directly.

R · complete S3 class
# 1) Constructor: creates the structure and attaches the class label
create_account <- function(holder, balance = 0, transactions = numeric(0)) {
  stopifnot(is.character(holder), is.numeric(balance))
  obj <- list(holder = holder, balance = balance, transactions = transactions)
  class(obj) <- "account"      # <- here the S3 class is born
  obj
}

# 2) print method: how the object looks when you type it
print.account <- function(x, ...) {
  cat("Account holder:", x$holder, "\n")
  cat("Current balance: $", format(x$balance, big.mark = ".", decimal.mark = ","), "\n")
  invisible(x)              # best practice: print returns the object invisibly
}

# 3) summary method: a computed summary, distinct from print
summary.account <- function(object, ...) {
  n <- length(object$transactions)
  cat("Summary of", object$holder, "'s account", "\n")
  cat("  Recorded transactions:", n, "\n")
  cat("  Balance:                $", object$balance, "\n")
  if (n > 0) cat("  Average transaction:    $", round(mean(object$transactions), 2), "\n")
  invisible(object)
}

# A custom method to deposit (returns a new account: functional style)
deposit <- function(x, ...) UseMethod("deposit")   # <- your own generic
deposit.account <- function(x, amount, ...) {
  x$balance <- x$balance + amount
  x$transactions <- c(x$transactions, amount)
  x
}
R · dispatch in action
a1 <- create_account("Ana Torres", balance = 1000)
a1                    # when you type the object, R dispatches to print.account
Account holder: Ana Torres Current balance: $ 1.000
R · continued
a1 <- deposit(a1, 500)
a1 <- deposit(a1, 250)
summary(a1)          # dispatches to summary.account
Summary of Ana Torres's account Recorded transactions: 2 Balance: $ 1750 Average transaction: $ 375

Notice the key detail: you never wrote print.account(a1) or summary.account(a1). You wrote a1 and summary(a1), and R did the routing by reading class(a1). That's exactly what lm, tibble, and thousands of objects do: they integrate into the language by contributing methods to existing generics.

R6: when you need mutable state

S3 and S4 are functional: when you "modify" an object you actually create a copy (that's why deposit() returned a new account). R6 breaks that rule: its objects are mutable by reference, as in Python or Java. A $deposit() method changes the object in place, without returning a copy. That's why R6 is the basis of modern Shiny and of everything that needs to keep live state: connections, caches, counters, state machines.

R · R6 (mutable state)
library(R6)

Account <- R6Class("Account",
  public = list(
    holder = NULL,
    balance = 0,
    initialize = function(holder, balance = 0) {
      self$holder <- holder
      self$balance <- balance
    },
    deposit = function(amount) {
      self$balance <- self$balance + amount   # mutates in place, no copy
      invisible(self)                          # allows chaining
    }
  )
)

a2 <- Account$new("Beatriz", 1000)
a2$deposit(500)$deposit(250)              # chained, mutates the same object
a2$balance
#> [1] 1750

Rule of thumb: use S3 by default (it's the idiomatic choice). Move up to S4/S7 only if you need formal validation and strict inheritance. Reserve R6 for what really needs to mutate state. When in doubt, the right answer is almost always S3.

S7: the future that unifies S3 and S4

S7 (2024) was born to end the eternal "informal S3 vs. formal S4" dilemma. It offers explicit definition of classes and properties with validation (like S4) but with clean syntax and compatibility with existing S3 dispatch. You define it with new_class(), properties are declared with types, and methods are registered with method(). ggplot2 4.0 has already migrated to S7. It's still young, but it's the official direction of formal OOP in R.

R · S7 (a glimpse)
library(S7)

Account <- new_class("Account", properties = list(
  holder  = class_character,
  balance = class_numeric
))

# Register a method for the print generic (S7 interoperates with S3)
method(print, Account) <- function(x, ...) {
  cat("S7 account for", x@holder, "- balance:", x@balance, "\n")
}

a3 <- Account(holder = "Carlos", balance = 900)
a3
#> S7 account for Carlos - balance: 900

Best practices for OOP in R: always write a constructor as the single entry point to your objects (never assign class() by hand all over the place); validate inputs in the constructor with stopifnot(); provide at least a print method so your object displays nicely; and document what fields it has. A well-made S3 object feels "native" to the language.

Common mistakes: (1) writing print.mycase with a typo in the class name: R doesn't warn you, the method simply never gets used and falls to the default. (2) Forgetting invisible(x) in print, which clutters the console. (3) Confusing class(x) <- "foo" (S3) with believing R will validate the structure: S3 validates nothing, that discipline is on you.

Guided exercise

Create an S3 class called "portfolio" that stores the investor's name and a named vector of values per asset (e.g. c(stocks = 5000, bonds = 3000)). Write: a constructor create_portfolio(), a print.portfolio method that lists each asset, and a summary.portfolio method that shows the total value and the highest-weighted asset. Verify dispatch by creating a portfolio and invoking print and summary without calling the methods by their full name.

View solution

Step 1 — Constructor. Takes the name and the named vector, validates, and labels the class.

R · solution
create_portfolio <- function(investor, assets) {
  stopifnot(is.character(investor),
            is.numeric(assets), !is.null(names(assets)))
  obj <- list(investor = investor, assets = assets)
  class(obj) <- "portfolio"
  obj
}

# Step 2 — print: lists each asset with its value
print.portfolio <- function(x, ...) {
  cat("Portfolio of", x$investor, "\n")
  for (nm in names(x$assets))
    cat("  -", nm, ": $", x$assets[[nm]], "\n")
  invisible(x)
}

# Step 3 — summary: total and highest-weighted asset
summary.portfolio <- function(object, ...) {
  total <- sum(object$assets)
  top <- names(which.max(object$assets))
  cat("Total value:  $", total, "\n")
  cat("Highest weight:", top,
      "(", round(100 * max(object$assets) / total, 1), "% )\n")
  invisible(object)
}

# Step 4 — test dispatch
p <- create_portfolio("Diana", c(stocks = 5000, bonds = 3000, cash = 2000))
p            # dispatches to print.portfolio
summary(p)   # dispatches to summary.portfolio

Expected output of summary(p):

Total value: $ 10000 Highest weight: stocks ( 50 % )

Notice that which.max returns the index of the maximum and names() retrieves its label: this is how you identify the dominant asset without manual loops.

Challenge

Extend the portfolio class with your own generic rebalance() that takes target weights (e.g. c(stocks = 0.6, bonds = 0.4)) and returns a new portfolio with the values redistributed while keeping the total. Then reimplement the entire class in R6 with a $rebalance() method that mutates the object in place, and compare: how does the functional flow (S3, returns a copy) differ from the mutable one (R6, changes in place)? Write two sentences explaining when you'd prefer each.

In short: R has four object systems. S3 dominates 90% of the language: it's informal, based on class() and generic dispatch (UseMethod reads the class and calls generic.class). S4 adds formality, R6 adds mutable state (the basis of Shiny), and S7 (2024) unifies the formal approach. The core skill is understanding that summary(model) ends up in summary.lm because R routes by class: master that, and you understand how almost all of R is built.

Package development

Packaging your code is the best way to reuse, document, test, and share it. A package isn't reserved for CRAN: it can live only on your machine or inside your company. With devtools and usethis the workflow is surprisingly smooth.

By the end you'll know: why packaging improves the quality of your code; the anatomy of a package (DESCRIPTION, NAMESPACE, R/, man/, tests/); the development cycle with devtools/usethis (load_all → document → test → check); documenting with roxygen2 and testing with testthat; and you'll have built a mini-package with a documented function and its test.

Why package your code

When you copy and paste the same function across scripts, sooner or later you'll have three slightly different versions and no record of which one is the good one. A package imposes structure and discipline with four concrete benefits: reuse (install once, use in any project with library()), document (each function has its help page, accessible with ?), test (automatic tests that warn you if you break something), and share (a colleague installs with one line from GitHub). The mental model: a package is your code turned into a tool with a contract, not a pile of loose scripts.

The development cycle with devtools and usethis

The division of labor is clear: usethis sets up (creates files, activates infrastructure) and devtools runs the cycle (load, document, test, check). These are the commands you'll use 95% of the time.

R · devtools/usethis workflow
library(devtools)          # also loads usethis

# --- Initial setup (once only) ---
create_package("~/mypackage")   # creates the structure and opens the project
use_mit_license()               # license (required for sharing)
use_r("vat")                    # creates and opens R/vat.R
use_testthat()                  # activates the test infrastructure
use_test("vat")                 # creates tests/testthat/test-vat.R

# --- Development cycle (you repeat this endlessly) ---
load_all()    # simulates library(mypackage): loads your code into memory
document()    # generates man/*.Rd and NAMESPACE from the roxygen2 comments
test()        # runs ALL the tests with testthat
check()       # full CRAN-style verification (errors, warnings, notes)
install()     # installs the package into your local library

The shortcut you'll use most: load_all() (Ctrl+Shift+L in RStudio) reloads your package in seconds without reinstalling it. It's your fast loop: edit R/, load_all(), test in the console, repeat. Save check() for before you share.

Anatomy of a package

A package is a folder with files in agreed-upon places. You don't need to create them by hand: usethis generates them. But you should understand what each one does.

ElementWhat it is
DESCRIPTIONMetadata: name, version, author, dependencies (Imports). The package's ID card.
NAMESPACEWhich functions it exports (are public) and what it imports from other packages. Don't edit it by hand: document() generates it from your @export tags.
R/Your source code: the .R files with the functions.
man/Help in .Rd format, generated by roxygen2. It's what you see with ?function.
tests/The automatic tests (testthat). They run with test().

Documenting with roxygen2

You write the documentation above the function, as special comments that start with #'. When you run document(), roxygen2 translates those comments into the help page (man/) and updates the NAMESPACE. Huge advantage: code and documentation live together, so it's hard for them to drift out of sync.

R · R/vat.R with roxygen2
#' Compute the price with VAT included
#'
#' Applies a VAT rate to a base price and returns the total.
#'
#' @param price Numeric. Base price before tax.
#' @param rate Numeric. VAT rate as a fraction (default 0.19 = 19%).
#' @return Numeric. The price with VAT added.
#' @examples
#' with_vat(1000)          # 1190
#' with_vat(1000, 0.05)    # 1050
#' @export
with_vat <- function(price, rate = 0.19) {
  stopifnot(is.numeric(price), is.numeric(rate), rate >= 0)
  price * (1 + rate)
}
TagWhat it's for
@paramDescribes an argument (one per parameter).
@returnWhat the function returns.
@examplesRunnable example code; check() runs it to verify it works.
@exportMakes the function public (it appears in NAMESPACE). Without it, the function is internal.

Testing with testthat

A test is code that verifies your function does what it promises. The structure is always the same: a test_that("description", { ... }) containing expectations (expect_*). If an expectation fails, test() tells you the line and the reason. Writing tests feels like extra work until the first time a test saves you from shipping a bug.

R · tests/testthat/test-vat.R
test_that("with_vat computes 19% by default", {
  expect_equal(with_vat(1000), 1190)
})

test_that("with_vat accepts other rates", {
  expect_equal(with_vat(1000, 0.05), 1050)
  expect_equal(with_vat(0), 0)
})

test_that("with_vat rejects invalid inputs", {
  expect_error(with_vat("thousand"))  # non-numeric price
  expect_error(with_vat(100, -0.1))   # negative rate
})
R · result of test()
test()
== Testing test-vat.R =========================== [ FAIL 0 | WARN 0 | SKIP 0 | PASS 5 ]
ExpectationVerifies
expect_equal(x, y)That x equals y (with numeric tolerance).
expect_identical(x, y)Strict equality, no tolerance.
expect_error(expr)That the expression throws an error (useful for validations).
expect_true(x) / expect_length(x, n)Logical and length conditions.

Publishing the documentation with pkgdown

When your package matures, pkgdown generates a complete website (function reference, vignettes, home page) from the documentation you already wrote. It's what produces those polished tidyverse sites.

R · pkgdown
usethis::use_pkgdown()      # configures pkgdown in the project
pkgdown::build_site()       # generates the site in docs/

Best practice: declare every dependency in DESCRIPTION with usethis::use_package("dplyr") and call external functions as dplyr::filter() (never rely on the user having run library). Never edit NAMESPACE or man/ by hand: they are generated. Run check() before sharing and aim for 0 errors, 0 warnings, 0 notes.

Common mistakes: (1) forgetting document() after changing an @export: your new function isn't exported and "disappears". (2) Using library(otherpackage) inside R/: that does not declare the dependency; use @importFrom or the package:: prefix. (3) Writing tests that depend on files with absolute paths on your machine: they fail on any other computer.

Guided exercise

Build a mini-package called utilities with one documented function called celsius_to_fahrenheit(c) that converts degrees Celsius to Fahrenheit (formula: c * 9/5 + 32). Document it with roxygen2 (include @param, @return, @examples, @export) and write at least two tests with testthat. Leave the package passing test() with no failures.

View solution

Step 1 — Create the skeleton in the console:

R · setup
library(devtools)
create_package("~/utilities")
use_mit_license()
use_r("temperature")     # creates R/temperature.R
use_testthat()
use_test("temperature")  # creates tests/testthat/test-temperature.R

Step 2 — Write the function in R/temperature.R:

R · R/temperature.R
#' Convert degrees Celsius to Fahrenheit
#'
#' @param c Numeric. Temperature in degrees Celsius.
#' @return Numeric. The equivalent temperature in Fahrenheit.
#' @examples
#' celsius_to_fahrenheit(0)    # 32
#' celsius_to_fahrenheit(100)  # 212
#' @export
celsius_to_fahrenheit <- function(c) {
  stopifnot(is.numeric(c))
  c * 9/5 + 32
}

Step 3 — Write the tests in tests/testthat/test-temperature.R:

R · test-temperature.R
test_that("water reference points", {
  expect_equal(celsius_to_fahrenheit(0), 32)
  expect_equal(celsius_to_fahrenheit(100), 212)
})

test_that("accepts vectors and rejects text", {
  expect_equal(celsius_to_fahrenheit(c(0, 100)), c(32, 212))
  expect_error(celsius_to_fahrenheit("cold"))
})

Step 4 — Generate the help and test:

R · cycle
document()   # generates man/celsius_to_fahrenheit.Rd and updates NAMESPACE
load_all()   # loads the package
test()       # should report PASS 4, FAIL 0
[ FAIL 0 | WARN 0 | SKIP 0 | PASS 4 ]

Now ?celsius_to_fahrenheit shows your help and celsius_to_fahrenheit(37) returns 98.6. You have a real package, documented and tested.

Challenge

Extend utilities with a second function fahrenheit_to_celsius() (the inverse) and add a test that verifies the round trip: expect_equal(fahrenheit_to_celsius(celsius_to_fahrenheit(25)), 25). Then configure pkgdown with use_pkgdown(), generate the site with build_site(), and run check(); adjust whatever is needed until you get 0 errors, 0 warnings, 0 notes.

In short: packaging turns loose scripts into a reusable, documented, tested tool. The flow is create_package → use_r/use_test → edit → load_all → document → test → check → install. The key anatomy: DESCRIPTION (metadata), NAMESPACE (generated), R/ (code), man/ (generated help), tests/ (tests). roxygen2 documents alongside the code, testthat protects against regressions, and pkgdown publishes the site.

Consuming and creating APIs

APIs let R talk to web services: weather, finance, social networks, AI models. With httr2 you consume other people's APIs; with plumber you turn your own R code into an API that others can call.

By the end you'll know: how to consume a REST API with httr2 (build the request, authenticate, handle retries and pagination, parse the response); how to protect your keys with environment variables; and how to expose your own model as an API with plumber using #* @get/@post annotations.

Mental model: an API is a question/answer contract over HTTP

Think of an API as a service window: you send a request (a URL with parameters, maybe an authentication key) and the server returns a response (usually JSON, with a status code that says whether it went well). All work with httr2 follows that script: you build the request piece by piece with chainable verbs (req_*), run it with req_perform(), and extract the body with resp_*. Chaining with |> makes the request read like a recipe.

Consuming a public API (httr2)

Real example: querying a city's current weather with the free, public API from Open-Meteo (no key required, ideal for practice). We pass it coordinates and ask for the current temperature.

R · httr2 · weather
library(httr2)

resp <- request("https://api.open-meteo.com/v1/forecast") |>
  req_url_query(
    latitude = 4.61,       # Bogota
    longitude = -74.08,
    current = "temperature_2m"
  ) |>
  req_user_agent("guia-r-ejemplo") |>
  req_retry(max_tries = 3) |>       # retries on transient failures
  req_perform()

resp_status(resp)          # 200 = all good
#> [1] 200

data <- resp_body_json(resp)
data$current$temperature_2m
#> [1] 18.4

The verbs you'll see again and again: request() starts the request with the base URL; req_url_query() adds parameters to the URL; req_headers() adds headers; req_perform() runs it; and on the response side, resp_status() gives the HTTP code and resp_body_json() converts the JSON into an R list.

Authentication, rate limits, and retries

Most serious APIs require a key. It's almost always sent as a Bearer token in the Authorization header. httr2 has a dedicated verb, req_auth_bearer_token(). On top of that, servers enforce rate limits (how many requests per minute) and respond with 429 Too Many Requests if you go over: req_retry() honors the Retry-After header and retries with exponential backoff.

R · httr2 · authentication + retries
resp <- request("https://api.finanzas.example/v1/precio") |>
  req_url_query(symbol = "AAPL") |>
  req_auth_bearer_token(Sys.getenv("FINANZAS_API_KEY")) |>  # key from environment
  req_retry(
    max_tries = 5,
    is_transient = \(r) resp_status(r) %in% c(429, 500, 503),
    backoff = \(i) 2 ^ i                # waits 2,4,8,16... seconds
  ) |>
  req_perform()

price <- resp_body_json(resp)$precio

Key security (critical): never write the key in your code or push it to Git. Store it in .Renviron (a single line FINANZAS_API_KEY=abc123) and read it with Sys.getenv("FINANZAS_API_KEY"). Add .Renviron to .gitignore. A key leaked in a public repo can cost you real money in minutes. Edit the file comfortably with usethis::edit_r_environ() and restart R so it loads.

Pagination

When an API returns many results, it splits them across pages. You have to request page after page until none are left. httr2 offers req_perform_iterative(), but the manual pattern with a loop is easy to understand and control.

R · httr2 · manual pagination
base <- request("https://api.datos.example/v1/registros") |>
  req_auth_bearer_token(Sys.getenv("MI_API_KEY"))

all_records <- list()
page <- 1
repeat {
  resp <- base |>
    req_url_query(page = page, per_page = 100) |>
    req_perform()
  body <- resp_body_json(resp)
  if (length(body$results) == 0) break     # no more data
  all_records <- c(all_records, body$results)
  page <- page + 1
}
length(all_records)   # total records accumulated

Tip: before crawling 500 pages, try just one and inspect the response structure with str(resp_body_json(resp), max.level = 2). Understanding the shape of the JSON saves you hours of trial and error. And be a good citizen: respect the rate limit (sometimes a small Sys.sleep() between pages is enough).

Creating your own API (plumber)

plumber turns R functions into HTTP endpoints by adding special #* comments above each function. It's the direct path to serving a model: you train in R and expose the prediction as a service any application can call. The mental model is the same contract, but now you are the service window.

R · plumber · api.R
library(plumber)

# Previously trained model (simplified here to a formula)
predict_price <- function(area, bedrooms) {
  50000 + as.numeric(area) * 1200 + as.numeric(bedrooms) * 8000
}

#* Check that the API is alive
#* @get /health
function() {
  list(status = "ok", time = Sys.time())
}

#* Predict the price of a home
#* @param area Area in m2
#* @param bedrooms Number of bedrooms
#* @get /predict
function(area, bedrooms = 2) {
  price <- predict_price(area, bedrooms)
  list(
    estimated_price = round(price),
    currency = "COP",
    inputs = list(area = as.numeric(area),
                  bedrooms = as.numeric(bedrooms))
  )
}

#* Predict from a JSON body (POST)
#* @post /predict
function(req) {
  data <- req$body
  list(estimated_price = round(
    predict_price(data$area, data$bedrooms)))
}
R · launch the API
# From the R console, in the project folder:
library(plumber)
pr("api.R") |> pr_run(port = 8000)
# The API is now listening on http://localhost:8000
# Automatic interactive documentation at http://localhost:8000/__docs__/

With the API running, anyone can query it. From another R session, closing the loop with httr2:

R · consume your own API
library(httr2)
resp <- request("http://localhost:8000/predict") |>
  req_url_query(area = 80, bedrooms = 3) |>
  req_perform()
resp_body_json(resp)
$estimated_price [1] 170000 $currency [1] "COP" $inputs $inputs$area [1] 80 $inputs$bedrooms [1] 3

Best practice when serving models: always include a /health endpoint for monitoring; validate and convert the parameters (they arrive as text, hence the as.numeric()); return JSON with a clear structure; and for production deploy behind a reverse proxy (nginx) or in a Docker container. Never execute arbitrary code that comes in the request.

Common mistakes: (1) forgetting that URL parameters arrive as strings: area + 1 fails if you don't convert with as.numeric(area). (2) Not handling the HTTP status: a 404 or 500 response also carries a body, just not the one you expect; check resp_status() before parsing. (3) Putting the key in the code "just to test" and forgetting to remove it before the commit.

Guided exercise

Consume the public Open-Meteo API to get the current temperature of three cities (use their coordinates) and build a data.frame with columns city and temperature. Wrap the query in a function weather(lat, lon) that returns the temperature, handle retries with req_retry(), and check the status before reading the body.

View solution

Step 1 — Reusable function. Build the request, check the status, and extract the temperature.

R · solution
library(httr2)

weather <- function(lat, lon) {
  resp <- request("https://api.open-meteo.com/v1/forecast") |>
    req_url_query(latitude = lat, longitude = lon,
                  current = "temperature_2m") |>
    req_user_agent("guia-r-ejercicio") |>
    req_retry(max_tries = 3) |>
    req_perform()
  stopifnot(resp_status(resp) == 200)      # abort if not 200
  resp_body_json(resp)$current$temperature_2m
}

Step 2 — Iterate over the cities. A data.frame with coordinates and mapply to apply the function row by row.

R · solution (cont.)
cities <- data.frame(
  city = c("Bogota", "Medellin", "Cartagena"),
  lat = c(4.61, 6.25, 10.39),
  lon = c(-74.08, -75.56, -75.51)
)

cities$temperature <- mapply(weather, cities$lat, cities$lon)
cities[, c("city", "temperature")]

Result (values change depending on when you run the query):

city temperature 1 Bogota 18.4 2 Medellin 24.1 3 Cartagena 30.7

Key points: the function isolates the network logic, stopifnot(resp_status(...) == 200) avoids parsing error responses, and mapply applies the query to each city without writing an explicit loop.

Challenge

Extend the plumber API so the /predict endpoint validates its inputs: if area is not numeric or is negative, respond with HTTP status 400 and a clear error message (use plumber's res argument: res$status <- 400). Then write an httr2 client that calls the endpoint with an invalid value and confirms it receives the 400. As a bonus, protect the API with your own API key header that you validate inside a #* @filter.

In short: an API is a question/answer contract over HTTP. With httr2 you build the request by chaining verbs (request → req_url_query → req_auth_bearer_token → req_retry → req_perform) and extract data with resp_body_json; authenticate with tokens read from .Renviron, never from the code. With plumber you expose your own code as endpoints using #* @get/@post annotations and launch it with pr_run(). Together they close the loop: you consume other people's services and publish your own.

Go deeper: httr2 — official site · httr2: wrapping APIs · plumber — documentation · Open-Meteo (practice API)
