Advanced Level
Home/Advanced Level/Scraping

Web Scraping

When data isn't available through an API, you can extract it from the HTML with rvest. Scraping isn't magic: it's reading the same HTML your browser sees and keeping the pieces you care about. Always do it ethically and legally.

By the end you'll know: how to download a page with read_html(), locate elements with CSS selectors using your browser's inspector, extract text and tables with html_text2() and html_table(), read attributes such as links with html_attr(), navigate sites with session(), decide when a site needs chromote, and —above all— scrape while respecting robots.txt, terms of service, and privacy.

Mental model: HTML is a tree

A web page is a document with a tree structure: the <html> tag contains <body>, which contains <div>, which contains <table>, and so on. Scraping boils down to three unchanging steps: (1) download that tree, (2) point at the branches you care about with a CSS selector, and (3) pull the text, table, or attribute from those branches. All of rvest revolves around those three verbs.

The key piece is the CSS selector: a mini-grammar for saying "I want the elements that meet this condition." The most common ones: p (every <p> tag), .precio (elements with class="precio"), #total (the element with id="total"), table.data (tables with class data), div.card > h2 (an h2 that is a direct child of a div.card), and a[href] (links that have an href attribute).

How to find the selector with the inspector

Don't guess selectors: read them off the browser. Open the page, right-click the value you want, and choose Inspect (or press F12). The developer tools panel opens with the HTML highlighted on the exact element. There you can see its tag, its class, and its id. Pro tip: in that panel, right-click the node → Copy → Copy selector gives you a CSS selector ready to paste. Another technique: press Ctrl+F inside the inspector and type a candidate selector; the browser highlights how many elements it matches, so you can refine it before touching R.

Tip: prefer selectors based on semantic class (.precio, .titulo-producto) over long paths like div > div > span:nth-child(3). Fragile paths break the moment the site changes a layout box; semantic classes usually survive redesigns.

The essential rvest functions

read_html() downloads and parses. html_elements() (plural) returns all the nodes matching the selector; html_element() (singular) returns only the first —or NA if there's none— and is handy for extracting one field per row without misaligning the data. On those nodes you apply the extractors: html_text2() (clean text, respecting line breaks the way a human sees them; almost always better than the old html_text()), html_table() (turns a <table> into a data.frame), and html_attr() (reads an attribute, typically "href" for links or "src" for images).

R · rvest
library(rvest)

page <- read_html("https://ejemplo.com/productos")

# Plural: a vector with EVERY match
titles <- page |> html_elements(".producto-titulo") |> html_text2()
prices <- page |> html_elements(".precio")          |> html_text2()

# Attribute: each product's link
links <- page |> html_elements(".producto-titulo a") |> html_attr("href")

# An entire table at once
tbl   <- page |> html_element("table") |> html_table()

data <- data.frame(title = titles, price = prices, link = links)

The misalignment bug: if you use html_elements() separately for title and price and some product has no price, the vectors end up with different lengths and data.frame() fails or —worse— pairs them up wrong. Robust solution: iterate row by row. First select each complete "card" with html_elements(".producto") and, within each one, use html_element() (singular) for each field; that way, if one is missing, you get NA in its place and everything stays aligned.

Robust row-by-row extraction (the pattern that actually scales)

R · rvest + purrr
library(rvest)
library(dplyr)

page <- read_html("https://ejemplo.com/productos")

# 1) One node per product (the full "card")
cards <- page |> html_elements(".producto")

# 2) Within each card, extract each field with the SINGULAR form
data <- tibble(
  title = cards |> html_element(".producto-titulo") |> html_text2(),
  price = cards |> html_element(".precio")          |> html_text2(),
  link  = cards |> html_element("a")                |> html_attr("href")
)

# 3) Cleanup: strip the currency symbol and convert to number
data <- data |>
  mutate(price_num = readr::parse_number(price, locale = readr::locale(decimal_mark = ",")))
# A tibble: 3 × 4 title price link price_num <chr> <chr> <chr> <dbl> 1 Mechanical keyboard $ 129.900 /prod/teclado 129900 2 Wireless mouse $ 59.900 /prod/mouse 59900 3 Monitor 27" $ 899.000 /prod/monitor 899000

Notice the analyst's logic: the price arrived as text ("$ 129.900") because in HTML everything is text. A value is only useful once you convert it to its real type. parse_number() extracts the number while ignoring symbols; watch out for the locale: in Spanish the dot is usually a thousands separator, not a decimal point.

Sessions and forms

Many sites require navigation (following links, keeping cookies) or submitting a search form. That's what session() is for: it behaves like a browser with memory, storing cookies between requests and letting you move forward with session_jump_to() or back with session_back(). For forms, html_form() reads the form, html_form_set() fills its fields, and session_submit() sends it.

R · rvest · sessions
library(rvest)

# We open a session, identifying ourselves with an honest user-agent
ua <- "UniversityResearch (contact: youremail@ejemplo.edu)"
s  <- session("https://ejemplo.com/buscar",
              httr::user_agent(ua))

# We read the page's search form
form <- s |> html_element("form") |> html_form()

# We fill a field and submit it
search <- form |> html_form_set(q = "R statistics")
result <- session_submit(s, search)

# On the results page we apply normal rvest
titles <- result |> html_elements(".resultado h3") |> html_text2()

Ethics and legality: not optional

Rules that aren't up for negotiation: (1) respect robots.txt (the site declares there which paths it forbids to bots). (2) read the terms of service; some prohibit scraping and that exposes you legally. (3) throttle your frequency with Sys.sleep() so you don't overload the server —scraping at full speed is, in effect, a small denial-of-service attack. (4) identify yourself with a real user-agent and a contact. (5) never extract personal data without a legal basis (habeas data / GDPR). (6) if there's an official API, use it: it's more stable, faster, and explicitly allowed.

R · polite scraping
# Check robots.txt before anything else
library(polite)   # a polite layer over rvest

session <- bow("https://ejemplo.com",
               user_agent = "UniversityResearch (youremail@ejemplo.edu)")
session   # prints whether the site allows the path and the recommended delay

# scrape() automatically respects robots.txt and waits between requests
tbl <- scrape(session) |> html_element("table") |> html_table()

# Manual alternative: explicit pause on each iteration of a loop
for (url in urls) {
  page <- read_html(url)
  # ... extract ...
  Sys.sleep(2)   # 2 seconds of courtesy between requests
}

JavaScript-rendered pages: if you can see the value in the browser when inspecting but read_html() returns an "empty" HTML, the content is painted by JavaScript after the page loads. rvest only sees the initial HTML. In that case you need a real browser driven by code: chromote (modern, based on headless Chrome) or RSelenium. Quick trick to detect it: search for the value with Ctrl+U (view source); if it's not there but does appear in the inspector, it's dynamic content.

Best practice: save the downloaded HTML to disk (write_html()) while developing, so you don't re-request the page on every test; wrap downloads in tryCatch() in case a URL fails; verify the selector returns what you expect before launching the full loop; and document the scraping date, because a site's HTML changes without warning.

Guided exercise

Wikipedia has a table of countries by population. Extract that table ethically, keep the country and population columns, and turn it into a clean data.frame with population as a number. Hint: use html_element("table.wikitable") and html_table().

View solution

Step 1 — Ethics first. Wikipedia allows moderate scraping and publishes its data under a free license; even so, we identify ourselves and don't hammer the server in a loop.

R · solution
library(rvest)
library(dplyr)

url <- "https://es.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anexo:Pa%C3%ADses_por_poblaci%C3%B3n"

# Step 2 — Download the page
page <- read_html(url)

# Step 3 — Locate the table (the 'wikitable' class is standard on Wikipedia)
tbl <- page |>
  html_element("table.wikitable") |>
  html_table()

# Step 4 — Keep the useful columns and clean up
population <- tbl |>
  select(country = 2, population = 3) |>    # adjust indices to the actual table
  mutate(population = readr::parse_number(population)) |>
  filter(!is.na(population)) |>
  arrange(desc(population))

head(population)
# A tibble: 6 × 2 country population <chr> <dbl> 1 India 1428600000 2 China 1425700000 3 United States 339900000 4 Indonesia 277500000 5 Pakistan 240500000 6 Nigeria 223800000

Step 5 — Interpretation. What arrived as text with thousands separators and footnotes ("India[a]") became workable numbers. The filter(!is.na()) drops header or total rows that parse_number() couldn't read. Now you can plot, sum by continent, or join with other data.

Challenge

Choose a news site that allows scraping according to its robots.txt. Extract the front-page headlines with their links, record the date and time of the extraction, and implement a pause of at least 2 seconds between requests. Bonus: wrap each download in tryCatch() so a broken link doesn't bring down the whole process, and detect whether the page uses JavaScript by comparing what you see in the browser with what read_html() returns.

In short: scraping is three steps —download with read_html(), point with CSS selectors you read from the inspector, and extract with html_text2()/html_table()/html_attr()—. Extract row by row with the singular form so you don't misalign data, use session() and html_form() for sites that require navigating or searching, and turn to chromote when the content is painted by JavaScript. But the first thing and the last thing is ethics: robots.txt, terms of service, limited frequency, an honest user-agent, and no personal data. If there's an official API, it always wins.

Go deeper: rvest.tidyverse.org (official documentation, with vignettes on selectors, sessions, and forms), the polite package for respectful scraping by default, and the web scraping chapter of R for Data Science (r4ds.hadley.nz).

Advanced Level
Home/Advanced Level/Machine Learning

Machine Learning

R has a mature ML ecosystem. The modern framework is tidymodels, which unifies the workflow with a consistent grammar: no matter the algorithm, the steps and the vocabulary are always the same.

By the end you'll know: why data is split into training and test sets, how to assemble the full tidymodels workflow (initial_split → recipe → parsnip → workflow → tune → yardstick), what overfitting is and how cross-validation detects it, what data leakage is and why preprocessing goes inside the workflow, and how to tune hyperparameters without fooling yourself. All with a complete, evaluated classification pipeline.

The mental model: five verbs, always in order

Every supervised ML project follows the same choreography. If you memorize it, any algorithm fits into it: (1) split the data so you can evaluate honestly, (2) preprocess with a reproducible recipe, (3) specify the model (which algorithm, which engine, which mode), (4) train by joining recipe and model into a workflow, and (5) evaluate with metrics on data the model never saw. Tuning is one more loop from 3 to 5 searching for the best hyperparameters.

The whole reason tidymodels exists is that each of those steps used to live in a separate package, with incompatible argument names. Switching from randomForest to xgboost forced you to rewrite everything. In tidymodels, switching algorithms means changing a single line: set_engine(). That consistency is what makes the framework so productive.

Step 1 — Split: the train/test discipline

This is the moral principle of ML: evaluating a model on the same data you trained it on is cheating. A flexible enough model can memorize the training set and score 100% there, yet still fail spectacularly on new data. That's why we set a test set aside from the start and treat it as sacred: we don't look at it, we don't touch it, we don't tune anything with it until the very end. It's our honest simulation of the "real world."

R · rsample
library(tidymodels)

# We use 'penguins' from modeldata: predict the penguin's species
data(penguins, package = "modeldata")
penguins <- na.omit(penguins)

set.seed(123)
# strata = species keeps the class proportions in both sets
split <- initial_split(penguins, prop = 0.75, strata = species)
train <- training(split)
test  <- testing(split)

# Folds for cross-validation (within the training set)
set.seed(234)
folds <- vfold_cv(train, v = 5, strata = species)

Tip: the strata argument is gold when classes are imbalanced. Without it, a random initial_split could leave almost all cases of a rare class in the test set. Stratifying guarantees that the proportion of each species is the same in training and test.

Step 2 — Preprocess with a recipe (and avoid data leakage)

A recipe (recipe) is a list of transformations —normalizing, creating dummy variables, imputing missing values, reducing dimensions— that are learned from the training set and applied identically to the test set. This is where one of the most treacherous ML mistakes lives: data leakage.

Data leakage: if you normalize using the mean and standard deviation of the entire dataset (train + test) before splitting, or if you impute missing values with the global mean, you're leaking information from the test set into training. Your metrics will come out optimistic and dishonest. The rule: any transformation that learns parameters from the data (mean, scale, PCA components) must be estimated using the training set only. Putting it in the recipe inside the workflow guarantees this happens automatically on every cross-validation fold.

Scale before PCA/clustering: techniques like PCA or k-means rely on distances, and a variable measured in thousands (income) dominates another measured in single units (age) purely because of its scale. Normalize (step_normalize) before step_pca or any distance-based method; forgetting this completely distorts the result.

R · recipes
rec <- recipe(species ~ bill_length_mm + bill_depth_mm +
              flipper_length_mm + body_mass_g + island,
              data = train) |>
  step_impute_median(all_numeric_predictors()) |>   # numeric missing values
  step_dummy(all_nominal_predictors()) |>           # 'island' → 0/1 variables
  step_normalize(all_numeric_predictors())          # mean 0, sd 1

Step 3 — Specify the model with parsnip

parsnip separates what model you want from which engine trains it. You declare the type (rand_forest, logistic_reg, boost_tree...), the engine (set_engine), and the mode (set_mode, "classification" or "regression"). The hyperparameters you want to tune you mark with tune().

R · parsnip
model_rf <- rand_forest(
    trees = 500,
    mtry  = tune(),      # number of variables per split: we'll tune this
    min_n = tune()       # minimum observations per leaf
  ) |>
  set_engine("ranger", importance = "impurity") |>
  set_mode("classification")

Step 4 — Join into a workflow

The workflow packages recipe + model into a single object. That union is what prevents data leakage: when you train the workflow, the recipe is fit after each fold is split off, never before.

R · workflows
wf <- workflow() |>
  add_recipe(rec) |>
  add_model(model_rf)

Step 5 — Tune with cross-validation

Cross-validation (k-fold) is the tool for choosing hyperparameters without touching the test set. It splits the training set into k folds; it trains on k-1 and validates on the remaining one, rotating. Each hyperparameter combination gets k evaluations, and we average them. This measures "out-of-sample" performance many times, and the variance across folds tells us whether the model is stable.

R · tune
set.seed(345)
grid_res <- tune_grid(
  wf,
  resamples = folds,
  grid      = 10,                              # 10 combinations to try
  metrics   = metric_set(accuracy, roc_auc)
)

# Which combination was the best by AUC?
show_best(grid_res, metric = "roc_auc", n = 3)
# A tibble: 3 × 8 mtry min_n .metric .estimator mean n std_err .config <int> <int> <chr> <chr> <dbl> <int> <dbl> <chr> 1 3 11 roc_auc hand_till 0.995 5 0.00212 Preprocessor1_Model07 2 2 21 roc_auc hand_till 0.994 5 0.00255 Preprocessor1_Model03 3 4 6 roc_auc hand_till 0.993 5 0.00301 Preprocessor1_Model09

Analyst's reading: the AUC hovers around 0.99 in the top three, and the std_err across folds is tiny. That signals an easy problem and a stable model —the three penguin species separate almost perfectly by bill and flipper measurements—. We pick the best combination and finalize the workflow with it.

The final step: train on all of train and evaluate ONCE on test

R · finalize
# Fix the best hyperparameters
best <- select_best(grid_res, metric = "roc_auc")
wf_final <- finalize_workflow(wf, best)

# last_fit trains on ALL of 'train' and evaluates on 'test' — just once
final_fit <- last_fit(wf_final, split,
                      metrics = metric_set(accuracy, roc_auc, f_meas))
collect_metrics(final_fit)
# A tibble: 3 × 4 .metric .estimator .estimate .config <chr> <chr> <dbl> <chr> 1 accuracy multiclass 0.976 Preprocessor1_Model1 2 f_meas macro 0.975 Preprocessor1_Model1 3 roc_auc hand_till 0.998 Preprocessor1_Model1

Overfitting: never evaluate on the same data you trained on. The classic sign is excellent performance on training and poor performance in cross-validation or on test. If you see that gap, your model memorized noise instead of learning the pattern: simplify it (less depth, more regularization, a larger min_n) or get more data. Cross-validation metrics (≈0.995) and test metrics (≈0.976) being similar is exactly what we want to see: the model generalizes.

Best practice: always set set.seed() for reproducibility; put all preprocessing in the recipe (never outside it); use strata when splitting and when resampling; tune hyperparameters with cross-validation, never with the test set; and touch the test set exactly once, with last_fit(), at the very end.

Guided exercise

Repeat the full workflow but change the algorithm to multinomial logistic regression (multinom_reg with the "nnet" engine) on the same penguins dataset. Since it's a linear model with no hyperparameters to tune here, skip tune_grid: train directly with cross-validation using fit_resamples() and compare its AUC with the random forest's.

View solution

Step 1 — Reuse recipe and data. We reuse the same split, rec, and folds from before; only the model specification changes.

R · solution
library(tidymodels)

# Step 2 — Specify the linear model
model_log <- multinom_reg() |>
  set_engine("nnet") |>
  set_mode("classification")

# Step 3 — New workflow with the SAME recipe
wf_log <- workflow() |>
  add_recipe(rec) |>
  add_model(model_log)

# Step 4 — Evaluate with cross-validation (no tuning)
set.seed(456)
res_log <- fit_resamples(
  wf_log,
  resamples = folds,
  metrics   = metric_set(accuracy, roc_auc)
)
collect_metrics(res_log)
# A tibble: 2 × 6 .metric .estimator mean n std_err .config <chr> <chr> <dbl> <int> <dbl> <chr> 1 accuracy multiclass 0.984 5 0.00681 Preprocessor1_Model1 2 roc_auc hand_till 0.999 5 0.00048 Preprocessor1_Model1

Step 5 — Interpretation. Multinomial logistic regression matches (or slightly beats) the random forest on this problem. Key analyst lesson: more complex isn't better by default. When the classes separate linearly, a simple, fast, interpretable model is the right choice. Save forests and boosting for nonlinear relationships that a linear model can't capture.

Challenge

Build a tidymodels regression (not classification) workflow to predict body_mass_g from the rest of the penguins variables. Use boost_tree with the "xgboost" engine, tune at least two hyperparameters (trees and learn_rate) via cross-validation, and report RMSE and R² on the test set with last_fit(). Does it improve on a simple linear regression lm()?

In short: tidymodels turns ML into a repeatable choreography of five verbs —split, preprocess, specify, train, evaluate— with cross-validation tuning in the middle. The non-negotiable discipline is to set the test set aside from the start and not look at it until last_fit(), put all preprocessing in the recipe to avoid data leakage, and use cross-validation —not the test set— to choose hyperparameters. Switching algorithms means changing one line; the workflow is always the same.

Go deeper: tidymodels.org (official tutorials and reference for each package), the book Tidy Modeling with R (tmwr.org) by Kuhn and Silge —the definitive reference for the workflow—, and the models chapter of R for Data Science (r4ds.hadley.nz).

Advanced Level
Home/Advanced Level/Supervised

Supervised models

They learn from labeled data to make predictions. If the response is numeric → regression; if it's categorical → classification. Here you'll meet the family of algorithms and —most importantly— how to read their metrics without fooling yourself.

By the end you'll know: how to tell regression from classification, when to use linear models (lm, glm) versus trees, random forest (ranger), and boosting (xgboost), what each metric measures (RMSE/MAE/R² for regression; accuracy, precision, recall, F1, and AUC-ROC for classification), how to read a confusion matrix, and why accuracy is misleading with imbalanced classes.

The family of algorithms: from simplest to most powerful

Mental model for choosing: start simple and move up in complexity only if the data justifies it. Linear models (lm for regression, glm(family = binomial) for binary classification) are interpretable and fast: each coefficient tells you how much the response changes per unit of the predictor. Decision trees capture nonlinear relationships and interactions, but a single one overfits. Random forest averages hundreds of trees over different samples, reducing variance —robust and low-maintenance—. Boosting (xgboost) builds trees sequentially, each one correcting the previous one's errors: it usually wins on accuracy, at the cost of more hyperparameters and a risk of overfitting if left unchecked.

R
# Linear and logistic regression (base R)
lm(mpg ~ wt + hp, data = mtcars)                  # numeric response
glm(am ~ mpg + wt, data = mtcars, family = binomial)  # logistic (0/1)

# Trees, random forest, boosting
library(ranger);  ranger(Species ~ ., data = iris)   # random forest
library(xgboost)  # high-performance gradient boosting

# In tidymodels, the same model is declared like this:
# rand_forest()  |> set_engine("ranger")  |> set_mode("classification")
# boost_tree()   |> set_engine("xgboost") |> set_mode("classification")

Correlation ≠ causation: a model predicting well doesn't mean its predictors cause the response. A model can exploit a spurious correlation (ice cream sales and drownings rise together... because both rise with the heat). Predicting and explaining are different goals: a large coefficient indicates association in your data, not a lever you can pull in the real world.

Regression metrics: how far off am I?

In regression we measure the distance between predicted and actual. RMSE (root mean squared error) is in the units of the response and penalizes large errors more —sensitive to outliers—. MAE (mean absolute error) is the typical error in the original units, more robust to outliers. R² is the proportion of variance explained (0 to 1): 0.85 means the model explains 85% of the variability.

Classification metrics: the table you should memorize

They all come from the confusion matrix, which cross-tabulates actual against predicted in four cells: true positives (TP), false positives (FP), true negatives (TN), and false negatives (FN).

MetricFormulaWhat it answersWhen it matters
Accuracy(TP+TN)/totalWhat fraction did I get right?Balanced classes
PrecisionTP/(TP+FP)Of what I flagged positive, how much really was?A false positive is costly
Recall (sensitivity)TP/(TP+FN)Of the actual positives, how many did I catch?A false negative is costly
F12·(P·R)/(P+R)Precision-recall balanceImbalanced classes
AUC-ROCarea under the ROC curveDoes it separate the classes well at every threshold?Comparing models, ranking

Tip: to keep them straight: precision answers "when I say yes, am I right?" and recall answers "of all the real yeses, how many did I find?". Bank fraud: you want high recall (don't let frauds slip through). Spam filter: guard precision (don't send legitimate mail to spam). There's almost always a trade-off between the two, and F1 sums it up.

The accuracy trap with imbalanced classes

The accuracy trap: with imbalanced classes (99% negatives), a model that always says "negative" has 99% accuracy and is completely useless: it doesn't detect a single positive case. Recall would be 0 and F1 would be 0, giving it away instantly. With imbalance, ignore accuracy and look at recall, F1, AUC-ROC, and the confusion matrix.

A real example: train and evaluate a classifier

We use a deliberately imbalanced case to see the trap in action: detecting the minority class in a dataset where only 8% of the cases are positive.

R · tidymodels
library(tidymodels)

# Dataset with strong class imbalance (~8% positives)
data(hpc_data, package = "modeldata")   # illustrative example

set.seed(2024)
split <- initial_split(df, prop = 0.75, strata = class)
train <- training(split); test <- testing(split)

model <- rand_forest(trees = 500) |>
  set_engine("ranger") |>
  set_mode("classification")

fit <- workflow() |>
  add_formula(class ~ .) |>
  add_model(model) |>
  fit(data = train)

# Class predictions AND probabilities (for AUC)
pred <- fit |>
  predict(test) |>
  bind_cols(predict(fit, test, type = "prob")) |>
  bind_cols(test |> select(class))

# Confusion matrix
conf_mat(pred, truth = class, estimate = .pred_class)
Truth Prediction positive negative positive 31 18 negative 27 424
R · metrics
# A handful of metrics at once
multi <- metric_set(accuracy, precision, recall, f_meas)
multi(pred, truth = class, estimate = .pred_class,
      event_level = "first")

# AUC-ROC needs the probability, not the class
roc_auc(pred, truth = class, .pred_positive, event_level = "first")
# A tibble: 5 × 3 .metric .estimator .estimate <chr> <chr> <dbl> 1 accuracy binary 0.910 2 precision binary 0.633 3 recall binary 0.534 4 f_meas binary 0.579 5 roc_auc binary 0.889

Analyst's interpretation. The 0.91 accuracy looks excellent... until you look at the rest. The recall of 0.53 confesses that the model misses almost half of the real positives (27 false negatives out of 58 positives). If "positive" were fraud or a disease, letting half slip away is serious. The precision of 0.63 says that of every 10 alarms, nearly 4 are false. The F1 of 0.58 sums up that mediocre performance on the class that matters —far from the 0.91 accuracy that would have fooled us—. The AUC of 0.89 is more encouraging: it indicates the model ranks cases well by probability, so lowering the decision threshold (flagging positive at less than 50% probability) would raise recall at the cost of precision. That's the analyst's lever against imbalance: move the threshold according to how much each type of error costs.

Common mistakes: reporting only accuracy with imbalanced classes; computing AUC by passing it the predicted class instead of the probability; confusing precision with recall; and choosing the model by its performance on training. Also watch event_level: it defines which factor level is the "positive" one; if you set it backwards, precision and recall come out swapped.

Best practice: always report several metrics and the confusion matrix, not a single number; under imbalance prioritize recall/F1/AUC and consider resampling (SMOTE, downsampling) or adjusting the threshold; choose the metric before seeing the results, guided by the real cost of each error; and validate on data the model didn't see.

Guided exercise

With the 4 cells of the example's confusion matrix (TP=31, FP=18, FN=27, TN=424), compute precision, recall, and F1 by hand for the "positive" class. Then imagine a lazy model that predicts "negative" for everyone: what would its accuracy, recall, and F1 be? Connect the result to the accuracy trap.

View solution

Step 1 — The real model. Precision = TP/(TP+FP) = 31/(31+18) = 31/49 = 0.633. Recall = TP/(TP+FN) = 31/(31+27) = 31/58 = 0.534. F1 = 2·(0.633·0.534)/(0.633+0.534) = 0.579. They match yardstick's output.

Step 2 — The lazy model. Total = 31+18+27+424 = 500. Real positives = 58; real negatives = 442. If it always predicts "negative": it gets the 442 negatives right and misses the 58 positives. Accuracy = 442/500 = 0.884.

R · verification
TP <- 31; FP <- 18; FN <- 27; TN <- 424
precision <- TP / (TP + FP)                 # 0.633
recall    <- TP / (TP + FN)                 # 0.534
f1        <- 2 * precision * recall / (precision + recall)  # 0.579

# Lazy model: never predicts positive
acc_lazy <- (FN + TN) / (TP + FP + FN + TN)   # 0.884
# lazy model's TP = 0  =>  recall = 0  =>  F1 = 0
c(precision, recall, f1, acc_lazy)
[1] 0.6326531 0.5344828 0.5794393 0.8840000

Step 3 — The moral. The lazy model scores 0.884 accuracy —almost as high as the real model (0.910)— but its recall and F1 are 0: it doesn't detect a single positive. Accuracy rewarded its laziness because negatives are abundant. That's why, under imbalance, recall and F1 are the honest judges.

Challenge

Train two classifiers on the same imbalanced dataset —a random forest (ranger) and a boosting model (xgboost)— and compare them by AUC-ROC, not accuracy. Then plot both ROC curves on a single chart with roc_curve() + autoplot(). Bonus: try rebalancing the classes with step_downsample() (from the themis package) inside the recipe and observe how recall and precision change. Which would you choose if a false negative costs ten times more than a false positive?

In short: regression predicts numbers (RMSE, MAE, R²), classification predicts categories (accuracy, precision, recall, F1, AUC-ROC). Start with interpretable linear models and move up to random forest or boosting only if the data calls for it. Every classification metric is born from the confusion matrix, so always read it. And remember the trap: with imbalanced classes, accuracy lies —recall, F1, and AUC are the ones that tell the truth—. Predicting well isn't explaining: correlation is not causation.

Go deeper: Tidy Modeling with R (tmwr.org) —chapters on performance metrics and model comparison—, the yardstick (tidymodels.org) reference for all the metrics, and tidymodels.org for end-to-end classification tutorials.

Advanced Level
Home/Advanced Level/Unsupervised

Unsupervised models

They find structure in data without labels: clustering and dimensionality reduction. No one tells you the "right answer"; the goal is to let the data itself reveal its hidden patterns.

By the end you'll know: why scaling before clustering or reducing dimensions is not optional; how to apply k-means and choose k with the elbow method and the silhouette; how to read a hierarchical clustering dendrogram; how to run a PCA and interpret the explained variance and a biplot; and how to explain in business terms what each technique reveals.

Mental model: group by similarity, compress without losing the essentials

In supervised learning you have a target column (y) and you learn to predict it. In unsupervised learning there is no y: you only have the feature columns (X) and you ask the algorithm to find order on its own. Two tasks dominate this world:

  • Clustering: gather observations that are similar to each other and different from the rest. Think of segmenting customers, grouping products, or detecting types of patients. The algorithm doesn't know what a "premium customer" is; it just measures distances and forms groups.
  • Dimensionality reduction: compress many correlated variables into a few that preserve almost all the variability. It's useful for visualizing high-dimensional data, removing redundancy, and speeding up later models. PCA (Principal Component Analysis) is the workhorse.

The word that ties it all together is distance. Almost all of these algorithms measure how far one observation is from another. And that's where problem number one appears: if one variable is in the thousands (income) and another in decimals (a rate), the large-scale one dominates the distance and the others become invisible. That's why, in clustering and PCA, scaling is a mandatory step, not a decoration.

The classic mistake: running kmeans() or prcomp() on the raw variables. Without scale(), the result reflects the units of measurement, not the real structure. Scale first, always.

K-means step by step

K-means looks for k centers (centroids) and assigns each point to the nearest center, repeating until the groups stabilize. It's fast and intuitive, but it has two demands: you decide k up front, and the result depends on where the centers start at random. For the latter, nstart is used: repeat the start many times and keep the best solution.

R
set.seed(123)

# 1) Scale: convert each variable to mean 0 and standard deviation 1
data <- scale(iris[, 1:4])            # scaling is crucial!

# 2) K-means with k = 3 and 25 different random starts
km <- kmeans(data, centers = 3, nstart = 25)

# 3) Inspect the result
km$size          # how many observations fell into each cluster
km$tot.withinss  # within-cluster inertia (lower = more compact groups)
[1] 50 53 47 # sizes of the 3 clusters [1] 138.8884 # within-cluster sum of squares (tot.withinss)

The km object stores the km$cluster vector (which group each row goes to), the km$centers (the centroids in standardized scale), and the inertia measures. Since iris does have a real species, we can check how well the natural groups match the species, even though the algorithm never saw that label:

R
# Compare clusters with the real species (for validation only)
table(cluster = km$cluster, species = iris$Species)
species cluster setosa versicolor virginica 1 50 0 0 2 0 39 14 3 0 11 36

Analyst's read: setosa separates perfectly (it's very distinct); versicolor and virginica overlap a little, which is correct because botanically they are similar. K-means recovered almost all the structure without labels. That's what you're after.

Choosing k: the elbow method and the silhouette

The big question is how many groups to ask for. There's no single mathematical answer; there are two complementary guides:

  • Elbow method: plot the within-cluster inertia (tot.withinss) for several values of k. At first it drops fast; there comes a point where adding more groups barely helps: that "elbow" suggests the reasonable k.
  • Silhouette: measures, for each point, how well it fits its cluster versus the nearest neighboring one. Values close to 1 are good; close to 0, ambiguous. The k that maximizes the average silhouette is usually a good choice.
R · factoextra
library(factoextra)

# Elbow method
fviz_nbclust(data, kmeans, method = "wss") +
  labs(title = "Elbow method")

# Average silhouette
fviz_nbclust(data, kmeans, method = "silhouette") +
  labs(title = "Average silhouette by k")
# fviz_nbclust(method = "silhouette") flags k = 2 as the maximum, # with k = 3 very close. The elbow is also visible at k = 2-3. # Decision: k = 3 if the business expects 3 segments; k = 2 if separation prevails.

The elbow and the silhouette rarely agree 100%. Treat them as advisors, not judges: the final number of segments must make operational sense. Three marketing campaigns are actionable; seventeen are not.

Hierarchical clustering and the dendrogram

K-means forces you to fix k before looking. Agglomerative hierarchical clustering does the opposite: it starts with each point as its own group and keeps merging the closest ones until everything ends up in a single tree. The result is a dendrogram, and you decide where to "cut" it to obtain the groups.

R
# We use mtcars, scaled, with car names as labels
cars <- scale(mtcars)

# 1) Matrix of Euclidean distances between cars
d <- dist(cars, method = "euclidean")

# 2) Hierarchical clustering with Ward linkage
hc <- hclust(d, method = "ward.D2")

# 3) Draw the dendrogram
plot(hc, main = "Clustering of cars (mtcars)", xlab = "", sub = "")

# 4) Cut the tree into 3 groups
groups <- cutree(hc, k = 3)
table(groups)
groups 1 2 3 16 7 9

The "ward.D2" linkage method is a solid default: it forms compact groups by minimizing the internal variance at each merge. The height at which two branches join indicates how different they are: low merges = very similar; high merges = very different groups. By cutting the dendrogram with cutree() you get clusters without having fixed k in advance.

K-means vs. hierarchical. K-means is fast and scales to millions of rows, but it requires k and assumes spherical groups. Hierarchical clustering doesn't need a prior k and produces the interpretable tree, but it costs a lot of memory (the distance matrix grows with the square of the rows). For large datasets: k-means. For exploring structure with a few hundred cases: hierarchical.

PCA: compressing many variables into a few

When you have dozens of correlated variables, many of them say "the same thing" in different words. PCA builds principal components: new variables (linear combinations of the originals) ordered from most to least explained variance. The first component captures the greatest possible variability; the second, the most of what remains while being perpendicular to the first; and so on. With 2-3 components you usually retain almost all the information.

R
# scale. = TRUE does the scaling internally: don't repeat scale() beforehand
pca <- prcomp(iris[, 1:4], scale. = TRUE)

summary(pca)      # variance explained by component
Importance of components: PC1 PC2 PC3 PC4 Standard deviation 1.7084 0.9560 0.38309 0.14393 Proportion of Variance 0.7296 0.2285 0.03669 0.00518 Cumulative Proportion 0.7296 0.9581 0.99482 1.00000

Direct interpretation: PC1 explains 73% of the total variability and PC2 23%; together, almost 96%. In other words, four flower measurements are summarized in two dimensions without losing almost anything. That's why PCA is so useful for visualizing: you project the data onto PC1-PC2 and see the whole structure on a plane.

R · factoextra
library(factoextra)

# Explained variance (scree plot)
fviz_eig(pca, addlabels = TRUE)

# Biplot: observations (points) + variables (arrows), colored by species
fviz_pca_biplot(pca,
                geom.ind = "point",
                col.ind  = iris$Species,
                addEllipses = TRUE,
                title = "PCA biplot of iris")
# The biplot shows setosa completely separated on the left; # versicolor and virginica together on the right. The arrows for # Petal.Length and Petal.Width point almost parallel to PC1: # they are the variables that most separate the species.

How to read a biplot: the points are the observations; the arrows are the original variables. Long arrows = well-represented variable; arrows pointing in the same direction = correlated variables; a 90° angle = no correlation. Here, Petal.Length and Petal.Width align with PC1, confirming that petal size is what most distinguishes the species.

Best practice: (1) always scale before clustering or reducing dimensions; (2) set set.seed() so k-means is reproducible; (3) use nstart = 25 or more to stabilize the result; (4) validate k with the elbow and the silhouette, but decide with business judgment; (5) report the cumulative variance of the PCA to justify how many components you keep.

Common mistakes: not scaling (the variable with the largest unit dominates everything); interpreting cluster numbers as if they had an order (the "cluster 1" is not better than the "3", they are arbitrary labels); believing PCA "removes variables" —in reality it combines them; and forcing high k values that fragment groups with no operational meaning.

Guided exercise

Using the USArrests dataset (arrests per 100,000 inhabitants in U.S. states), segment the states into crime groups. Scale the data, choose k with the elbow method and the silhouette, run k-means, and describe what characterizes each group. Then apply PCA and check how much variance the first two components explain.

View solution

Step 1 — Explore and scale. USArrests has 4 variables (Murder, Assault, UrbanPop, Rape) on very different scales, so scaling is mandatory.

R
set.seed(2024)
library(factoextra)

crime <- scale(USArrests)
head(crime, 3)

Step 2 — Choose k. Compare the elbow and the silhouette.

R
fviz_nbclust(crime, kmeans, method = "wss")
fviz_nbclust(crime, kmeans, method = "silhouette")  # suggests k = 2

Step 3 — Run k-means with k = 4 (a compromise between interpretability and detail) and describe the groups.

R
km <- kmeans(crime, centers = 4, nstart = 25)
fviz_cluster(km, data = crime)          # visualize the clusters on PC1-PC2

# Profile of each group on the original scale
aggregate(USArrests, by = list(cluster = km$cluster), FUN = mean)
cluster Murder Assault UrbanPop Rape 1 1 3.60 78.54 52.08 12.18 # low crime 2 2 13.94 243.62 53.75 21.41 # high violence, little urban 3 3 10.82 257.38 76.00 33.19 # high crime and urban 4 4 5.66 138.88 73.87 18.78 # medium, very urban

Step 4 — PCA. Confirm the dimensionality reduction.

R
pca <- prcomp(USArrests, scale. = TRUE)
summary(pca)$importance[ , 1:2]
fviz_pca_biplot(pca, repel = TRUE)
PC1 PC2 Standard deviation 1.574878 0.99486 Proportion of Variance 0.620060 0.24744 Cumulative Proportion 0.620060 0.86750

Conclusion: PC1 (62%) represents the general level of crime; PC2 (25%) the degree of urbanization. With two components you explain ~87% of the variability, and the clusters separate cleanly on that plane.

Challenge

Take the mtcars dataset, apply hierarchical clustering with three different linkage methods ("complete", "average", "ward.D2") and compare the dendrograms. Do all three agree on grouping the sports cars versus the sedans? Then project the groups onto the first two components of a PCA and decide which linkage+cut combination tells the clearest story. Justify your choice.

In short: unsupervised learning looks for structure without labels. K-means groups by proximity to centroids (fix k, use nstart, scale first); hierarchical clustering builds a tree you cut wherever you want; PCA compresses correlated variables into components ordered by explained variance. Scaling is the step you can't skip, and business judgment always validates the mathematical result.

Go deeper: factoextra documentation (visual clustering and PCA), the chapter of An Introduction to Statistical Learning (ISLR, free) on unsupervised methods, and the UC Business Analytics k-means guide.

Home/Advanced Level/Time series

Time series

Data ordered in time (monthly sales, prices, sensors). The modern ecosystem is Rob Hyndman's fpp3/fable, and its rule of thumb is simple: each value depends on the previous ones, so order rules.

By the end you'll know: why a time series is not just any table; how to convert data to a tsibble and plot it; how to distinguish trend, seasonality, and autocorrelation; how to decompose a series with STL; how to fit and compare ARIMA and ETS models with fable; how to generate and read a forecast with intervals; and why random cross-validation is forbidden in time series.

Mental model: the past conditions the future

In the data you've seen so far, each row was independent: you could shuffle them without losing information. In a time series that's false. December's sales depend on November's; today's temperature is similar to yesterday's. This dependence is called autocorrelation, and it's what makes forecasting possible: if nothing depended on the past, there would be nothing to predict.

Every series is thought of as the sum (or product) of three pieces:

  • Trend: the underlying long-term direction (is it growing, falling, stalling?).
  • Seasonality: patterns that repeat with a fixed period (more sales in December, more traffic on Mondays).
  • Residual (or remainder): what's left after removing trend and seasonality; ideally noise with no pattern.

Separating these pieces is called decomposing, and it's the first step of any serious analysis. The modern tool in R is the fpp3 trio: tsibble (the temporal data structure), feasts (visualization and decomposition) and fable (modeling and forecasting), all with tidyverse grammar.

From vector to tsibble

The classic ts object works, but a tsibble is a data frame that knows which is its time-index column. Let's convert AirPassengers (monthly air passengers, 1949-1960), the canonical example with marked trend and seasonality.

R · fable
library(fpp3)   # loads tsibble + feasts + fable + tidyverse

# Convert the classic ts to a tsibble with a monthly index (yearmonth)
series <- as_tsibble(AirPassengers) |>
  rename(passengers = value)

series
# A tsibble: 144 x 2 [1M] index passengers <mth> <dbl> 1 1949 Jan 112 2 1949 Feb 118 3 1949 Mar 132 4 1949 Apr 129 5 1949 May 121 # ... with 139 more rows

The [1M] indicates the interval is monthly. That metadata lets fable know the seasonal period is 12. Let's plot:

R
series |> autoplot(passengers) +
  labs(title = "Monthly air passengers", y = "Thousands of passengers")

# See the seasonality explicitly
series |> gg_season(passengers)    # one line per year, overlaid
# autoplot: clearly rising trend + regular annual oscillation. # gg_season: every year shows peaks in July-August (summer), # and the peak amplitude GROWS over the years -> multiplicative seasonality.

When the seasonal amplitude grows with the level of the series (as here), it helps to use a logarithmic transformation or let the model handle it. gg_season() and gg_subseries() are your best friends for seeing the seasonality before modeling.

STL decomposition

STL (Seasonal-Trend decomposition using Loess) separates the series into trend, seasonality, and residual in a robust and flexible way: the seasonality can evolve over time, and outliers don't ruin the trend. It's the recommended default method.

R · feasts
series |>
  model(STL(passengers ~ trend() + season(window = "periodic"))) |>
  components() |>
  autoplot()
# Four stacked panels: # passengers : original series # trend : smooth, rising curve (112 -> ~430) # season_year: annual wave with summer peaks # remainder : small residual, no obvious pattern

Analyst's read: if the remainder panel still shows structure (regular peaks, trend), the model didn't capture everything. Here the residual is practically noise, a sign of a good decomposition.

Autocorrelation: the signature of temporal dependence

The autocorrelation function (ACF) measures how much the series resembles itself lagged k periods. In seasonal data you'll see peaks at lags that are multiples of the period (12, 24...).

R
series |> ACF(passengers) |> autoplot() +
  labs(title = "Autocorrelation function (ACF)")
# ACF high and slowly decaying -> strong trend. # Upticks at lags 12 and 24 -> annual seasonality.

Modeling and forecasting: ARIMA and ETS

With fable you fit several models at once and compare them. Two families dominate:

  • ETS (Error, Trend, Seasonal): exponential smoothing. It models the series as a combination of level, trend, and seasonality that update with each observation. Excellent for series with clear seasonality.
  • ARIMA (AutoRegressive Integrated Moving Average): models the autocorrelation directly. fable's ARIMA() automatically chooses the optimal orders.
R · fable
# Fit ARIMA and ETS in a single call (automatic)
fit <- series |>
  model(
    arima = ARIMA(passengers),
    ets   = ETS(passengers)
  )

fit                    # mable table with one model per column
glance(fit)            # fit metrics: AIC, BIC, etc.
# A mable: 1 x 2 arima ets <model> <model> 1 <ARIMA(2,1,1)(0,1,0)[12]> <ETS(M,A,M)> # glance(): the ETS(M,A,M) usually gives the lowest AICc on this series, # capturing the multiplicative seasonality we saw earlier.

Now we forecast 24 months ahead. The prediction intervals (shaded) reflect the growing uncertainty: the farther out, the wider.

R · fable
# 24-month forecast of both models
fc <- fit |> forecast(h = 24)

fc |> autoplot(series, level = c(80, 95)) +
  labs(title = "2-year forecast", y = "Thousands of passengers")
# Chart: history + two forecast fans (arima and ets). # Both continue the rising trend and repeat the summer peaks, # with 80% and 95% bands that widen toward the future.

Why random cross-validation is forbidden

In tabular models you shuffle the rows and distribute them into folds at random. In time series that's cheating: you would train with data from the future to predict the past, something impossible in real life. It's called temporal data leakage and it produces deceptively good metrics.

The correct way is rolling-origin validation (time series cross-validation): train with the first n periods, predict the next one, advance the window and repeat. The test set is always after the training set.

R · fable
# 1) Simple temporal split: train up to 1958, test 1959-1960
train <- series |> filter(index < yearmonth("1959 Jan"))
test  <- series |> filter(index >= yearmonth("1959 Jan"))

fit <- train |> model(ets = ETS(passengers))
fit |> forecast(h = 24) |> accuracy(test)

# 2) Rolling-origin validation
series_cv <- series |>
  stretch_tsibble(.init = 100, .step = 1)
series_cv |>
  model(ETS(passengers)) |>
  forecast(h = 1) |>
  accuracy(series)
# accuracy() over the future test set: # .model .type RMSE MAE MAPE # ets Test 22.4 17.8 3.9% # The ~4% MAPE indicates forecasts with an average error of 4%: very reasonable.

Common mistakes: shuffling rows or using random createFolds() on a series (information leakage from the future); forgetting to declare the time index and losing the seasonality; extrapolating the forecast too many periods (uncertainty explodes); and ignoring the residual diagnostics. Always check with gg_tsresiduals() that the residual is white noise.

Best practice: visualize before modeling (autoplot, gg_season); decompose with STL to understand the structure; compare several models with glance() and choose by AICc or by error in temporal validation; report forecasts with intervals, never just the point; and always validate respecting the order of time.

Guided exercise

Use the USAccDeaths series (monthly accidental deaths in the U.S., 1973-1978). Convert it to a tsibble, visualize it, decompose it with STL, fit an ETS and an ARIMA, compare them, and forecast 12 months. Interpret whether there is seasonality and in which months the peaks occur.

View solution

Step 1 — Prepare and visualize.

R
library(fpp3)

deaths <- as_tsibble(USAccDeaths) |> rename(deaths = value)
deaths |> autoplot(deaths)
deaths |> gg_season(deaths)     # peaks in summer (July), troughs in winter

Step 2 — Decompose with STL. Confirm the annual seasonality.

R
deaths |>
  model(STL(deaths)) |>
  components() |>
  autoplot()

Step 3 — Fit and compare.

R
fit <- deaths |>
  model(ets = ETS(deaths), arima = ARIMA(deaths))
glance(fit) |> select(.model, AICc)
# .model AICc # ets 852.1 # arima 848.6 # ARIMA with the lowest AICc: preferred

Step 4 — Forecast and validate the residual.

R
fit |> select(arima) |> forecast(h = 12) |> autoplot(deaths)
fit |> select(arima) |> gg_tsresiduals()   # residual ~ white noise

Conclusion: there is clear annual seasonality with a maximum in July (more accidental deaths in summer) and a minimum in February. The ARIMA wins by AICc and its residual shows no remaining autocorrelation, so the 12-month forecast is reliable within its intervals.

Challenge

Download or simulate a monthly sales series with trend and seasonality. Implement a rolling-origin validation (stretch_tsibble()) comparing ETS, ARIMA, and a seasonal naive model (SNAIVE). Compute the RMSE of each at horizon 1 and 6. Which model wins in the short term and which in the long term? Argue why the naive model is a baseline that every serious forecast must beat.

In short: a time series is ordered dependence: each value rests on the previous ones. The professional workflow in R is fpp3: convert to a tsibble, visualize seasonality, decompose with STL, fit and compare ETS/ARIMA with fable, forecast with intervals, and validate respecting the temporal order. Random cross-validation leaks the future and is forbidden.

Go deeper: Forecasting: Principles and Practice (3rd ed., Hyndman & Athanasopoulos, free), the fable.tidyverts.org reference, and the full ecosystem at tidyverts.org.

Advanced Level
Home/Advanced Level/Econometrics

Econometrics in R

R is the language of choice for academic econometrics: robust regressions, panel data, instrumental variables, and publishable tables. The focus is not just to predict, but to estimate causal effects honestly.

By the end you'll know: what fixed effects are and why they control for omitted-variable bias; how to estimate panel models with fixest::feols including entity and time fixed effects; how to compute clustered and robust standard errors; how to estimate instrumental variables with the y ~ x | fe | endog ~ instr syntax; how to produce publishable regression tables with etable/modelsummary; and how to interpret coefficients without overstating causality.

Mental model: compare within, not between

The econometrician's great enemy is omitted-variable bias: if an unmeasured characteristic affects both the predictor and the outcome at once, your coefficient lies. Example: estimating the effect of education on wages. "Innate ability" is not observed, raises both, and would contaminate the estimate.

Fixed effects are the most elegant answer when you have panel data (the same entities observed over several periods: people by year, firms by quarter, countries by decade). The idea: instead of comparing different people, you compare each person with themselves over time. Everything that doesn't change within that person (their ability, their background, their character) is automatically eliminated. Adding time fixed effects does the same with shocks common to everyone in each year (a recession, a reform).

Entity fixed effects = "compare within the same unit". Time fixed effects = "remove what affects everyone in that period". Together, they isolate the variation you really care about.

fixest: the modern standard

The fixest package is today the first choice: it estimates models with multiple fixed effects at enormous speed and with clean syntax. Let's build a wage panel (people observed over several years) and estimate the return to education and experience.

R · fixest
library(fixest)
set.seed(42)

# Simulate a panel: 300 people x 6 years
n_id  <- 300; years <- 2015:2020
panel <- expand.grid(id = 1:n_id, year = years)

# Unobserved ability (fixed per person) that biases the naive OLS
ability <- rnorm(n_id, sd = 1)[panel$id]
panel$education  <- round(10 + ability + rnorm(nrow(panel)), 1)
panel$experience <- panel$year - 2015 + round(runif(nrow(panel), 0, 5))
# Log wage: true return to education = 0.08, to experience = 0.02
panel$log_wage <- 6 + 0.08*panel$education + 0.02*panel$experience +
                  1.5*ability + rnorm(nrow(panel), sd = 0.3)

First, the naive OLS (without fixed effects) overestimates the return to education because it also captures ability. Then, per-person fixed effects correct it:

R · fixest
# Naive OLS: biased by omitted ability
ols <- feols(log_wage ~ education + experience, data = panel)

# Fixed effects by person (id) and by year, errors clustered by person
fe <- feols(log_wage ~ education + experience | id + year,
            cluster = ~id, data = panel)

etable(ols, fe)     # comparison table ready to publish
ols fe Dependent Var.: log_wage log_wage Constant 5.98*** (0.09) education 0.34*** (0.01) 0.081*** (0.012) experience 0.02*** (0.004) 0.021*** (0.005) Fixed-Effects: ---------------- ---------------- id No Yes year No Yes _______________ ________________ ________________ S.E.: Clustered --- by: id Observations 1,800 1,800 R2 0.62 0.94 Within R2 -- 0.31 Signif.: *** 0.001 ** 0.01 * 0.05

Econometrician's interpretation: the naive OLS gives a "return" of 0.34 per year of education, absurdly high because it confuses education with ability. With fixed effects, the coefficient drops to 0.081, very close to the simulated true value (0.08). Lesson: without controlling for the unobserved, the number lies. The experience barely changes because it wasn't correlated with ability.

Standard errors: clustered and robust

Classic standard errors assume observations are independent and homoscedastic. In panel data that almost never holds: the observations of the same person are correlated across years. Ignoring it produces standard errors that are too small and false significance.

  • Clustered errors: allow arbitrary correlation within each group (person, firm, region). In fixest: cluster = ~id. It's the standard in panel data.
  • Heteroscedasticity-robust errors (HC): correct for non-constant variance. With feols, vcov = "hetero". With classic lm models, use sandwich + lmtest.
R
# In fixest: change the standard error type without re-estimating
summary(fe, vcov = "hetero")     # heteroscedasticity-robust
summary(fe, cluster = ~id)       # clustered by person (default here)

# With classic lm + sandwich/lmtest
library(sandwich); library(lmtest)
m  <- lm(log_wage ~ education + experience, data = panel)
coeftest(m, vcov = vcovHC(m, type = "HC1"))   # HC1 robust errors
# coeftest with HC1 robust errors: # Estimate Std. Error t value Pr(>|t|) # (Intercept) 5.981 0.104 57.5 <2e-16 *** # education 0.341 0.011 31.0 <2e-16 *** # experience 0.021 0.005 4.2 3e-05 *** # Robust errors adjust the inference without changing the coefficients.

Instrumental variables (IV)

When a regressor is endogenous (correlated with the error, for example education if some uncontrolled ability remains), fixed effects are not enough. The solution is instrumental variables: a variable that affects the endogenous regressor but not the outcome except through it. fixest expresses it with an additional vertical bar: endogenous ~ instrument.

R · fixest
# Syntax: y ~ exogenous | fixed_effects | endogenous ~ instrument
# Example: instrument education with "distance_to_university"
panel$distance <- rnorm(nrow(panel)) + 0.5*panel$education  # synthetic instrument

iv <- feols(log_wage ~ experience | year | education ~ distance,
            cluster = ~id, data = panel)
summary(iv)
# fitstat(iv, ~ ivf) returns the first-stage F statistic:
# if it's < 10, the instrument is weak and the estimate is unreliable.
fitstat(iv, ~ ivf1)
# TSLS estimation - Dep. Var.: log_wage # Endogenous: education | Instrument: distance # Estimate Std. Error t value # education 0.079 0.018 4.4 *** # experience 0.020 0.006 3.3 ** # F-test (1st stage), education: stat = 812.3 -> STRONG instrument

Weak instruments. An instrument is only useful if it strongly predicts the endogenous regressor (first-stage F > 10 as a rule of thumb) and if it is exogenous (it doesn't affect the outcome through another channel). This second condition can't be tested with data: it requires a theoretical argument. A weak or invalid instrument produces estimates worse than OLS.

plm: the classic panel approach

plm is the traditional reference. It offers the Hausman test to decide between fixed effects (within) and random effects (random), something an academic referee often asks for.

R · plm
library(plm)

# Declare the panel structure (index: person, time)
pdata <- pdata.frame(panel, index = c("id", "year"))

# Fixed effects (within) vs random effects (random)
fe_plm <- plm(log_wage ~ education + experience, data = pdata, model = "within")
re_plm <- plm(log_wage ~ education + experience, data = pdata, model = "random")

# Hausman test: fixed or random effects?
phtest(fe_plm, re_plm)   # p < 0.05 -> use fixed effects
# Hausman Test # chisq = 148.2, df = 2, p-value < 2.2e-16 # p < 0.05: random effects are rejected -> fixed effects are preferred.

Publishable tables with modelsummary

For reports and papers, modelsummary exports tables to HTML, LaTeX, Word, or Markdown with professional formatting, and fixest's etable generates LaTeX directly.

R · modelsummary
library(modelsummary)

modelsummary(
  list("OLS" = ols, "Fixed effects" = fe, "IV" = iv),
  stars = TRUE,
  gof_map = c("nobs", "r.squared", "within.r.squared"),
  output = "regressions.html"      # also .tex, .docx, .md
)

Common mistakes: interpreting a regression coefficient as causal without an identification strategy (fixed effects, IV, experiment); using classic standard errors in panel data (they inflate significance); confusing statistical significance with economic relevance; and presenting an instrument without checking its strength (first-stage F) or arguing its exogeneity.

Best practice: always declare the clustering level of your standard errors; report the naive OLS alongside the fixed-effects model to show how much it corrects; in IV show the first-stage F; interpret coefficients in interpretable units (here, elasticities since it's log wage); and be explicit about the causal assumptions you make.

Guided exercise

With the Grunfeld dataset from the plm package (investment of 10 firms over 20 years), estimate the effect of market value (value) and capital (capital) on investment (inv). Compare a pooled OLS against a model with firm and year fixed effects, using errors clustered by firm. Interpret how the coefficients change.

View solution

Step 1 — Load the data.

R
library(fixest)
data("Grunfeld", package = "plm")
head(Grunfeld)     # firm, year, inv, value, capital

Step 2 — Pooled OLS (ignores the panel structure).

R
pooled <- feols(inv ~ value + capital, data = Grunfeld)

Step 3 — Firm and year fixed effects, clustered by firm.

R
fe <- feols(inv ~ value + capital | firm + year,
            cluster = ~firm, data = Grunfeld)
etable(pooled, fe)
pooled fe inv (Dep. Var.) value 0.116*** (0.006) 0.110*** (0.015) capital 0.231*** (0.025) 0.310*** (0.052) Fixed-Effects: ---------------- -------------- firm No Yes year No Yes Observations 200 200 R2 0.81 0.94 Within R2 -- 0.77

Conclusion: the coefficient of value drops slightly (0.116 → 0.110) and that of capital rises (0.231 → 0.310) when controlling for the fixed differences between firms. The clustered standard errors are larger (more honest) than the pooled ones. The causal interpretation is more credible with fixed effects because each firm is compared with itself over time.

Challenge

Reproduce a difference-in-differences design with fixest::feols: create (or use) a panel where a subset of entities receives a "treatment" starting in a certain year. Estimate the effect with y ~ i(post_treatment, treated, ref = 0) | id + year and clustered errors. Plot the event study with iplot() to check the parallel-trends hypothesis before treatment. Discuss which identification assumption lets you call the coefficient a "causal effect".

In short: econometrics in R revolves around estimating effects, not just predicting. fixest::feols is the modern tool for panel data: fixed effects that absorb the unobserved, clustered/robust errors that give honest inference, and instrumental variables for endogeneity, all with publishable tables (etable, modelsummary). plm contributes the classic Hausman test. The final rule: no coefficient is causal without an identification strategy to justify it.

Go deeper: official fixest documentation, the CRAN Task View: Econometrics, the book Applied Econometrics with R (Kleiber & Zeileis, AER package), and the modelsummary reference for publishable tables.

Shiny: interactive web apps

Shiny turns your R analysis into an interactive web application, with no HTML or JavaScript required. It's the bridge between the analysis and the end user.

By the end you'll know: how to split an app into ui (what you see) and server (what gets computed); how to use the most common inputs and outputs; and how to master reactivity —reactive(), eventReactive(), observeEvent() and isolate()— so your app recomputes exactly what it needs, no more and no less.

The mental model: a living spreadsheet

Think of Shiny as a giant spreadsheet. In Excel, when you change cell A1, every formula that depends on A1 recalculates on its own; you don't re-run anything. Shiny works the same way: the inputs are the cells the user edits, the outputs are the cells with formulas, and R takes care of recomputing only whatever became out of date. The difference from a normal script runs deep: in a script you control the order of execution from top to bottom; in Shiny you declare dependencies and the engine decides when to run each piece. That's why it pays to stop thinking "first this, then that" and start thinking "this depends on that."

The two halves: UI and server

Every Shiny app has two halves. The UI (user interface) is a description of the page: which controls exist and where they go. The server is a function(input, output, session) that holds the logic: it reads from input$… and writes to output$…. shinyApp(ui, server) joins them and starts the local web server. Each user who opens the app gets their own isolated session: if two people move the slider, they don't step on each other.

R · app.R
library(shiny)

ui <- fluidPage(
  titlePanel("mtcars Explorer"),
  sidebarLayout(
    sidebarPanel(
      sliderInput("hp", "Minimum power (hp):",
                  min = 50, max = 350, value = 100)
    ),
    mainPanel(plotOutput("grafico"))
  )
)

server <- function(input, output) {
  output$grafico <- renderPlot({
    library(ggplot2)
    datos <- subset(mtcars, hp >= input$hp)   # reactive to input$hp
    ggplot(datos, aes(wt, mpg)) + geom_point(size = 3) + theme_minimal()
  })
}

shinyApp(ui, server)

Notice the pairing by id: in the UI you wrote sliderInput("hp", …) and plotOutput("grafico"); in the server you read input$hp and write output$grafico. The ids are the invisible wiring between the two halves. A classic mistake is typing input$HP or output$grafica: R is case-sensitive and won't warn you with a red error, the output simply stays blank.

Reactivity: the heart of Shiny. When the user moves the slider, everything that depends on input$hp recomputes on its own. Key concepts: reactive(), observeEvent(), eventReactive().

The most common inputs and outputs

The UI is assembled by combining *Input() functions (they collect data from the user) and *Output() functions (they reserve a slot where the server will draw). Each *Output() in the UI corresponds to a render*() in the server. These are the pairs you'll use 90% of the time:

In the UIWhat it collects / showsIn the server
sliderInput()A number or range via a slider—
numericInput() / textInput()Number or free text—
selectInput()Dropdown menu of options—
checkboxInput() / radioButtons()Yes/no or one option among several—
actionButton()A button that triggers an action—
plotOutput()A plotrenderPlot()
tableOutput() / DT::DTOutput()A table (static or interactive)renderTable() / DT::renderDT()
textOutput() / verbatimTextOutput()Text or console outputrenderText() / renderPrint()
plotly::plotlyOutput()An interactive plotplotly::renderPlotly()

The reactive graph: reactive(), eventReactive(), observeEvent(), isolate()

Reactivity is organized into three kinds of pieces. The inputs are the sources (the user changes them). The reactive expressions (reactive()) are intermediate computations that are cached and shared. The observers and outputs are the leaves of the graph: they consume values and produce an effect (draw, write, save). Shiny builds a dependency graph and only re-runs the branches that became "dirty."

reactive() — shared, lazy computation. Wraps a computation that several outputs use. It runs only when someone calls it (lazy) and caches the result until one of its dependencies changes (cache). It avoids repeating the same filtering or the same query in every render*():

R
server <- function(input, output) {
  # Recomputes when input$hp changes; shared across outputs
  datos_filtrados <- reactive({
    subset(mtcars, hp >= input$hp)
  })

  output$grafico <- renderPlot({
    ggplot(datos_filtrados(), aes(wt, mpg)) + geom_point()
  })
  output$n <- renderText({
    paste("Cars that qualify:", nrow(datos_filtrados()))
  })
}

Note the key detail: a reactive expression is called like a function, with parentheses: datos_filtrados(). If you write datos_filtrados without parentheses, you get the reactive object, not its value.

eventReactive() — computes only when a button is pressed. Sometimes you don't want the computation to fire with every keystroke, but only when the user decides. eventReactive() depends on an explicit trigger (usually an actionButton()) and ignores changes from the other inputs until it's pressed:

R
# UI: actionButton("calcular", "Calculate")
resultado <- eventReactive(input$calcular, {
  Sys.sleep(2)              # simulates an expensive computation
  mean(subset(mtcars, hp >= input$hp)$mpg)
})
output$prom <- renderText(resultado())

observeEvent() — side effects, not values. When you want to do something (show a notification, save to disk, update another input) instead of returning something, you use an observer. It doesn't produce a value that others can read; it runs code for its effect:

R
observeEvent(input$guardar, {
  write.csv(datos_filtrados(), "salida.csv", row.names = FALSE)
  showNotification("File saved", type = "message")
})

isolate() — read without depending. Inside a reactive context, reading input$x creates a dependency. If you want to check the current value of something but without its change triggering the recompute, you wrap it in isolate(). It's the tool for "use whatever title is there now, but only redraw when the data changes":

R
output$grafico <- renderPlot({
  datos_filtrados()                         # DOES trigger a redraw
  titulo <- isolate(input$titulo)           # is read, but does NOT trigger
  ggplot(datos_filtrados(), aes(wt, mpg)) + geom_point() + ggtitle(titulo)
})

A practical rule for choosing the piece: if it returns a value that others reuse, use reactive() (or eventReactive() if it should wait for a button). If it produces an effect (save, notify, update), use observeEvent(). The render*() functions are special cases of observers tied to an output.

Best practice: keep the server thin —the heavy logic goes in ordinary R functions, easy to test outside Shiny—; centralize each computation in a single reactive() instead of repeating it; and name the ids in consistent snake_case so you don't confuse input$fecha_inicio with input$fechaInicio.

Common mistake: putting code with side effects (saving files, sending emails, writing to a database) inside a render*() or a reactive(). Those pieces can run many times or not at all, depending on what the user looks at. Effects always go in observeEvent()/observe(), which run predictably.

Infinite reactivity: if inside an observer you update an input that in turn triggers that same observer, you create a loop that runs the app away. Break the cycle with isolate() or by gating the update with if.

For large apps, use Shiny modules (they encapsulate reusable UI+server) and the {golem} framework to structure them as a package. The book Mastering Shiny (free) is the reference.

Guided exercise

Start from the example app.R app. Add: (1) a selectInput("transmision", …) that lets you filter by automatic or manual transmission (column am: 0 = automatic, 1 = manual); (2) a textOutput("resumen") that shows how many cars meet both filters. Reuse a reactive() so the plot and the text share the same subset.

View solution

Step 1 — UI: add the dropdown to the sidebarPanel and a text slot in the mainPanel.

R
sidebarPanel(
  sliderInput("hp", "Minimum power (hp):", min = 50, max = 350, value = 100),
  selectInput("transmision", "Transmission:",
              choices = c("All" = "todas", "Automatic" = "0", "Manual" = "1"))
),
mainPanel(
  plotOutput("grafico"),
  textOutput("resumen")
)

Step 2 — server: centralize the filtering in a reactive() that combines both controls.

R
server <- function(input, output) {
  datos <- reactive({
    d <- subset(mtcars, hp >= input$hp)
    if (input$transmision != "todas") d <- subset(d, am == as.numeric(input$transmision))
    d
  })
  output$grafico <- renderPlot({
    ggplot(datos(), aes(wt, mpg)) + geom_point(size = 3) + theme_minimal()
  })
  output$resumen <- renderText({
    paste("Cars that meet the filters:", nrow(datos()))
  })
}

Step 3 — verify: when you change the slider or the dropdown, the plot and the text update at the same time because both read from the same datos(). Since the filtering lives in a single reactive(), it isn't recomputed twice: that's the gain of sharing the reactive expression.

Challenge

Turn the app into an "on-demand" tool: add an actionButton("aplicar", "Apply filters") and make the plot recompute only when it's pressed, using eventReactive(). Then add a "Download filtered data" button with downloadHandler() and an observeEvent() that shows a notification when it finishes. Think about it: why does the button avoid unnecessary expensive computations while the user adjusts several controls?

In short: a Shiny app is ui + server joined by ids. The reactive engine recomputes only what depends on an input that changed. Use reactive() for shared values, eventReactive() to wait for a button, observeEvent() for effects, and isolate() to read without depending. Keep the heavy logic in ordinary functions and the server thin.

Go deeper: Mastering Shiny (Hadley Wickham's free book, the definitive reference), the official documentation at shiny.posit.co and its gallery of examples with code, and the modules guide for large apps.
