Git and GitHub

Version control is non-negotiable in professional work: it tracks changes, enables collaboration, and is your safety net.

By the end you'll know: why version control replaces the analisis_final_v3_DEFINITIVO.R files; the basic flow (init, add, commit, push, pull, branches); how to enable Git in an R project with usethis; what your .gitignore should ignore; and how to collaborate with branches and pull requests, resolving a simple conflict.

Why Git, and the mental model

Without version control, your "history" is a folder full of copies with increasingly desperate names. Git solves three problems at once. Track: it saves a snapshot (commit) of the whole project whenever you decide to, with date, author and a message explaining the why; you can always return to any earlier snapshot. Collaborate: several people work on the same code without overwriting each other, each on their own branch, and then they merge. Safety net: if a change breaks something, you go back to the last healthy commit in seconds. The key mental model: Git doesn't save "the current file," it saves a timeline of states of the entire project, and you decide when to take each snapshot.

The basic flow

Git has three zones: the working directory (your files as they are), the staging area (what you'll include in the next snapshot) and the repository (the timeline of commits). add moves changes from the working directory to staging; commit turns staging into a permanent snapshot. push/pull sync that timeline with a remote like GitHub.

bash
git init                       # initialize repository
git status                     # see what changed and what's staged
git add .                      # stage changes
git commit -m "Initial analysis"
git remote add origin URL
git push -u origin main

git checkout -b nueva-feature  # create and switch to a branch
git checkout main              # go back to the main branch
git merge nueva-feature        # merge the branch into main
git pull                       # bring in changes from the remote

A typical glance at git status orients you without memorizing anything: it tells you which branch you're on, what's staged and what isn't.

On branch main Changes to be committed: (use "git restore --staged <file>..." to unstage) modified: analisis.R Changes not staged for commit: modified: README.md

Git from R (usethis)

You don't need the terminal for everyday work. The usethis package automates the setup from the R console, and RStudio ships with an integrated Git panel. This dramatically lowers the barrier to entry for anyone coming from statistics rather than software development.

R
library(usethis)
use_git()              # starts git in the project and makes the first commit
use_github()           # creates the repo on GitHub and connects it (needs a token)
git_sitrep()           # diagnostics: user, remote, GitHub token

The first time, set up your identity (it will appear in every commit) and create a token for GitHub. usethis guides you through both:

R
use_git_config(user.name = "Your Name", user.email = "you@email.com")
create_github_token()  # opens the browser to generate a token
gitcreds::gitcreds_set()  # stores the token securely

The .gitignore for R projects

Not everything should be versioned. Heavy data, temporary results and —above all— credentials never go to the repository. The .gitignore lists file patterns that Git should ignore. usethis::use_git_ignore() adds entries, and when creating an R project it's worth starting from a base like this:

bash
# .gitignore for R
.Rhistory          # console history
.RData             # workspace image
.Rproj.user/       # local RStudio settings
.Renviron          # environment variables with secrets
data/              # raw data, often heavy or confidential
*.csv              # (adjust to your case)
renv/library/      # libraries rebuildable from renv.lock

Never push credentials to Git. API keys, passwords and tokens don't belong in the code or in a versioned .csv: keep them in .Renviron (which is in .gitignore) and read them with Sys.getenv(). And remember: if you push a secret and later "delete" it in a subsequent commit, it's still there in the history. Consider it compromised and revoke it immediately.

Essential: add a .gitignore that excludes sensitive data, .Renviron, .Rhistory and large folders. usethis::use_git_ignore() helps. Make small commits with descriptive messages.

Commit hygiene

A good history reads like a logbook. Make small, atomic commits: one commit per idea, not a "saving everything from today." Write messages in the imperative mood that say the what and the why, not the how. Compare: "changes" says nothing; "Fix the unemployment-rate calculation (the expansion factor was missing)" saves you six months from now. Small commits also make reverting easier: if something broke, you know exactly which snapshot introduced it.

Avoid the junk-drawer commit: lumping a bug fix, a massive reformat and a new function into a single snapshot makes it impossible to review or revert surgically. If you've already mixed changes in staging, use git add -p to stage them in chunks.

GitHub workflow: branches and pull requests

On a team, the golden rule is don't work directly on main. Each task lives on its own branch; when it's ready, you open a pull request (PR): a merge proposal that others review and comment on before integrating. This keeps main always working and leaves a record of why each change went in.

bash
# 1. Start from an up-to-date main
git checkout main
git pull

# 2. Branch for the new task
git checkout -b feature/grafico-desempleo

# 3. Work and commit in small steps
git add R/grafico.R
git commit -m "Add unemployment-rate plot by department"

# 4. Push the branch and open the PR
git push -u origin feature/grafico-desempleo
# GitHub shows a link to open the Pull Request

After the review and the merge on GitHub, you go back to main, bring in what was merged and delete the already-integrated branch:

bash
git checkout main
git pull
git branch -d feature/grafico-desempleo

Resolving a basic conflict

A conflict happens when two branches change the same lines of a file and Git doesn't know which one wins. It's not an error: it's Git asking you for a decision. When you merge or pull, you'll see something like this inside the file:

bash
<<<<<<< HEAD
tasa <- ocupados / poblacion_activa
=======
tasa <- (ocupados / poblacion_activa) * 100
>>>>>>> feature/grafico-desempleo

Your job is to edit the file, leaving the correct version, delete the three markers (<<<<<<<, =======, >>>>>>>) and then close the merge:

bash
git add R/tasas.R      # marks the conflict as resolved
git commit             # closes the merge

Conflicts hurt less if you pull often and keep branches short: the less time a branch lives, the fewer chances it has of clashing with others' work. RStudio and VS Code highlight the conflict markers and offer buttons to pick each version.

Guided exercise

Create a versioned R project from scratch and take it to GitHub, without pushing sensitive data. Goal: a repository with at least two commits, a .gitignore that excludes .Renviron and data/, and a merged feature branch.

View solution

Step 1 — initialize with usethis. In an open RStudio project:

R
library(usethis)
use_git()                       # git init + first commit
use_git_ignore(c(".Renviron", "data/"))
git_sitrep()                    # confirms user and token

Step 2 — second commit. Create an analisis.R, save it and commit from RStudio's Git panel (or via the terminal):

bash
git add analisis.R
git commit -m "Add initial analysis script"

Step 3 — branch, change and merge.

bash
git checkout -b feature/limpieza
# you edit analisis.R...
git add analisis.R
git commit -m "Clean up column names"
git checkout main
git merge feature/limpieza
git branch -d feature/limpieza

Step 4 — publish on GitHub.

R
use_github()   # creates the remote repo and pushes

Check with git log --oneline that there are at least two commits and that data/ and .Renviron don't appear on GitHub. Done: a reproducible, versioned and secure project.

Challenge

Simulate a real collaboration with yourself: clone your repo into two different folders (as if they were two people). In each copy, edit the same line of a file with different values, commit, and push from the first one. From the second one, run pull: you'll trigger a conflict. Resolve it by hand, close the merge and push the result. Document in the commit message which version you kept and why.

In short: Git gives you history, collaboration and a safety net. The cycle is status → add → commit → push/pull. Work on branches and merge with pull requests; never directly on main on a team. Ignore data and secrets with .gitignore, make small commits with clear messages, and don't fear conflicts: they're a decision you make, not a failure.

Go deeper: Happy Git and GitHub for the useR (Jenny Bryan, the essential guide for R users), the usethis documentation and GitHub's official guide.

Production and deployment

Taking your work to production means others use it reliably and reproducibly.

By the end you'll know: which artifact deploys where (Shiny app, API, report, batch); why reproducibility is the central problem of production; how to pin versions with renv and contain the environment with Docker; and what the pillars are —pinned versions, contained environment, CI/CD and logging— that make your code run the same today, on another machine and a year from now.

The real problem: "it works on my machine"

An analysis that runs on your laptop but fails on the server isn't a finished analysis. The cause is almost always the same: environment differences —another version of R, other package versions, another operating system, other variables—. Deploying well is, above all, eliminating those differences. The mental model: you don't deploy "your code," you deploy your code plus the exact environment where it works, packaged together.

What you deploy and where

The destination depends on the artifact. Publishing an interactive app is not the same as serving an API or scheduling a nightly report:

What you deployWhere / how
Shiny appshinyapps.io, Posit Connect, or Docker on your server
API (plumber)Docker + any cloud (AWS, GCP, Azure)
Quarto reportsQuarto Pub, GitHub Pages, Netlify
Batch processescron / GitHub Actions running an .R script

To get started, the simplest route is usually the managed one: shinyapps.io publishes an app with a button in RStudio, and Quarto Pub or GitHub Pages host a report for free. When you need full control —system dependencies, scaling, privacy—, you make the jump to Docker, which is the common denominator of the whole table.

Reproducibility, step 1: renv

renv solves half of the problem: it pins the exact package versions of your project in a renv.lock file. Each project has its own isolated library, so updating a package in one project doesn't break another. The flow is three verbs:

R
renv::init()      # creates the isolated environment and detects packages used
renv::snapshot()  # writes renv.lock with the current versions
renv::restore()   # reinstalls EXACTLY what renv.lock says

The renv.lock file does go to Git (it's the recipe); the renv/library/ folder doesn't (it's rebuildable). Anyone who clones your repo and runs renv::restore() gets the same versions you have. That already eliminates the "it worked for me with the old dplyr version" class of bug.

Reproducibility, step 2: Docker

renv pins the packages, but not the version of R or the operating-system libraries. Docker closes that gap: it packages R, the system and your packages into an image that runs identically on any machine with Docker. The rocker/r-ver images ship a specific version of R ready to use. The renv + Docker combination is the reproducibility standard in R: renv pins the packages, Docker pins everything else.

Dockerfile
# Base image with a FIXED version of R (reproducible)
FROM rocker/r-ver:4.6.0

# System dependencies that R packages often require
RUN apt-get update && apt-get install -y --no-install-recommends \
    libcurl4-openssl-dev libssl-dev libxml2-dev \
    && rm -rf /var/lib/apt/lists/*

WORKDIR /app

# 1) Copy ONLY the recipe first: takes advantage of layer caching.
#    As long as renv.lock doesn't change, Docker won't reinstall packages.
COPY renv.lock renv.lock
RUN R -e "install.packages('renv')" \
    && R -e "renv::restore()"

# 2) Now the app code (changes often, goes at the end)
COPY . /app

# 3) Shiny port and startup command
EXPOSE 3838
CMD ["R", "-e", "shiny::runApp('/app', host = '0.0.0.0', port = 3838)"]

With that, you build and run the image like this, and the app becomes available at localhost:3838:

bash
docker build -t mi-app-shiny .
docker run -p 3838:3838 mi-app-shiny

The order of the Dockerfile lines matters because of layer caching: copying renv.lock and installing packages before copying the code means that, when only the code changes, Docker reuses the expensive layer (installing packages) and rebuilds in seconds instead of minutes.

The pillars of production

Beyond the tools, four principles set a serious deployment apart from a script hanging on a server:

  • Pinned versions. No "the latest version": R, packages and system are nailed down (renv.lock + image tag 4.6.0). Without this, a silent update breaks production on some random Tuesday.
  • Contained environment. The entire context travels inside the Docker image; the server only needs Docker installed, not R configured by hand.
  • CI/CD. Automate testing and deployment with GitHub Actions: every push runs the tests, builds the image and publishes it, with no manual steps that get forgotten.
  • Logging and monitoring. Record what happened and when. With logger or log4r you write events and errors; without logs, a production failure is a mystery with no clues.

Not pinning versions is the number-one cause of broken deployments. A FROM rocker/r-ver:latest or an install.packages() without renv means today's build and tomorrow's can bring different packages. And never put secrets (keys, tokens) inside the Dockerfile or the image: inject them at runtime with environment variables (docker run -e API_KEY=…) or secrets managers.

Test the clean image before trusting it: building it on your machine and having it work doesn't guarantee it works on another. Run docker build from a fresh clone of the repo; if it fails, you forgot to declare a dependency and it's better to find out now than on the server.

Pillars of production: pinned versions (renv), contained environment (Docker), automation (CI/CD with GitHub Actions), logging and monitoring. The goal: for it to run the same today, on another machine and a year from now.

Guided exercise

Take a minimal Shiny app (you can reuse the one from the Shiny section) and make it reproducible and contained: pin its packages with renv, write a Dockerfile based on rocker/r-ver:4.6.0, and build the image. Goal: bring the app up inside a container at localhost:3838.

View solution

Step 1 — pin packages. In the project folder, with the app in app.R:

R
renv::init()      # detects shiny, ggplot2, etc.
renv::snapshot()  # generates renv.lock

Step 2 — Dockerfile. Create a Dockerfile file next to the project:

Dockerfile
FROM rocker/r-ver:4.6.0
RUN apt-get update && apt-get install -y --no-install-recommends \
    libcurl4-openssl-dev libssl-dev libxml2-dev && rm -rf /var/lib/apt/lists/*
WORKDIR /app
COPY renv.lock renv.lock
RUN R -e "install.packages('renv')" && R -e "renv::restore()"
COPY . /app
EXPOSE 3838
CMD ["R", "-e", "shiny::runApp('/app', host = '0.0.0.0', port = 3838)"]

Step 3 — build and run.

bash
docker build -t mi-app-shiny .
docker run -p 3838:3838 mi-app-shiny

Step 4 — verify. Open http://localhost:3838 in the browser: the app runs inside the container, with the exact versions from renv.lock and R 4.6.0, independent of whatever you have installed on your system. That image is what you'd push to a server or the cloud.

Challenge

Automate the deployment with CI/CD. Create a GitHub Actions workflow (.github/workflows/deploy.yml) that, on every push to main, restores the environment with renv::restore(), builds the Docker image and publishes it to a registry (GitHub Container Registry or Docker Hub). Add a step that fails the build if renv::status() detects that renv.lock is out of date. Reflect: which manual steps did you just eliminate, and what human errors does that prevent?

In short: deploying is packaging code and environment. Choose the destination by the artifact (Shiny, API, report, batch). Pin the packages with renv and contain everything else with Docker on top of rocker/r-ver. Hold the system up with four pillars: pinned versions, contained environment, CI/CD and logging. Never use latest or put secrets in the image. The acid test: that it runs the same anywhere, today and a year from now.

Go deeper: the renv documentation, the Rocker Project (official Docker images for R), the Posit documentation (shinyapps.io and Posit Connect) and the Quarto publishing guides.

Best practices and project architecture

A professional project is readable, reproducible and maintainable. Structure matters as much as the code.

By the end you'll know: how to organize an R project with a standard folder structure, make it reproducible with renv and relative paths (here), apply the tidyverse style guide with styler and lintr, honor the DRY principle, safeguard your raw data, and orchestrate the whole workflow with targets so that anyone can clone the repository and run it without surprises.

The mental model: "clone and run"

The acid test of a professional project is simple: an outsider (or your future self six months from now) clones the repository, opens the .Rproj, runs one command, and everything works. If reproducing your analysis requires "fixing paths by hand", "installing some packages I don't quite remember", or "running the scripts in a certain secret order", the project isn't reproducible: it's an anecdote. The why is economic. In data science, 80% of a project's cost happens after you write it —when someone reads it, reviews it, audits it, or extends it. Every minute you invest in structure today saves hours tomorrow.

Recommended structure for a project

There's no single correct tree, but there is a widely shared convention in the R community. The idea is to separate by responsibility: data, functions, execution scripts, outputs and reports live in distinct, predictable folders.

project/
my_project/
├── my_project.Rproj     # RStudio project (anchors the root)
├── README.md            # what it does, how to install and run it
├── .gitignore           # ignores data/raw/, output/, .Rhistory, etc.
├── renv.lock            # exact versions of every package
├── renv/                # project-isolated library
├── _targets.R           # pipeline definition (orchestration)
├── data/
│   ├── raw/             # original data — SACRED, read only
│   └── processed/       # clean data generated by the code
├── R/                   # reusable functions (clean_data.R…)
├── scripts/             # 01_ingest.R, 02_clean.R, 03_model.R…
├── output/
│   ├── figures/         # exported charts
│   ├── tables/          # result tables
│   └── models/          # serialized models (.rds)
├── tests/               # tests with testthat
└── reports/             # Quarto reports (.qmd) and their output

Notice three details that separate an amateur from a professional: there's a README.md explaining how to get started, there's a .gitignore that avoids committing heavy data or session junk, and the scripts are numbered to communicate the execution order at a glance.

Reproducibility: running the same on any machine

Two silent enemies break reproducibility: absolute paths and package versions. Both have a one-line solution.

Relative paths with here, never setwd()

The cardinal sin is setwd("C:/Users/luis/Desktop/project"). That path exists only on your computer; on any other machine the script blows up. The solution is here::here(), which locates the project root (where the .Rproj lives) and builds paths relative to it.

R · portable paths
# BAD: only works on your machine
setwd("C:/Users/luis/OneDrive/project")
data <- read.csv("data/raw/sales.csv")

# GOOD: works on any machine that clones the repo
library(here)
data <- readr::read_csv(here("data", "raw", "sales.csv"))

here()  # prints the detected root
> here()
[1] "C:/Users/luis/projects/my_project"

Isolated environment with renv

renv gives each project its own package library and records the exact versions in renv.lock. That way, if your analysis worked with dplyr 1.1.4, whoever clones it will install that same version and not a future one that changed a function's behavior.

R · renv workflow
renv::init()      # 1. start renv in the project
# … you install and use packages as usual …
renv::snapshot()  # 2. freeze versions in renv.lock

# On another machine, after cloning the repo:
renv::restore()   # 3. reinstall EXACTLY those versions

The professional workflow is a cycle of three verbs: init once, snapshot every time you add or update packages, and restore when someone (or you) opens the project on a new machine.

Consistent style: code that reads like prose

The tidyverse style guide is the de facto standard in R: snake_case names, the assignment operator <- instead of =, spaces around operators, and lines that don't exceed 80 columns. The best part is that you don't have to memorize it: two packages apply it for you.

  • styler automatically reformats your code (indentation, spaces, line breaks).
  • lintr is a static reviewer: it changes nothing, but it flags code smells (unused variables, long lines, = where <- should be).
R · automatic styling
styler::style_file("scripts/02_clean.R")     # reformats the file
styler::style_dir("R")                        # reformats the whole folder
lintr::lint_dir("R")                          # reports style issues
scripts/02_clean.R:14:22: style: Use <-, not =, for assignment.
  total = sum(sales)
      ^

DRY: don't repeat yourself

The rule is simple: if you copy and paste the same block three times, turn it into a function and put it in R/. The why isn't aesthetic: duplicated code gets fixed in one place and breaks in three. A well-named function also documents the intent.

R · from repetition to function
# Repeated in every script → one function in R/utils.R
clean_currency <- function(x) {
  x |>
    stringr::str_remove_all("[$,]") |>
    as.numeric()
}

# Now, in any script:
source(here::here("R", "utils.R"))
sales <- mutate(sales, price = clean_currency(price))

Raw vs. processed data: raw data is sacred

Unbreakable rule: never overwrite a file in data/raw/. Raw data is the source of truth; every transformation must read from raw/ and write to processed/. If one day you discover a bug in the cleaning step, you want to be able to return to the original starting point. Treat data/raw/ as if it were read-only, even at the system-permissions level.

Serious mistake: opening sales.csv in Excel, "fixing" a couple of cells, and saving over it. You've just destroyed reproducibility and left no trace of the change. Every fix must live in code, inside a versioned script.

Orchestration with targets

As the project grows, running 01, 02, 03… by hand becomes fragile: did you change the cleaning step but forget to retrain the model? targets solves this. You define your workflow as a series of steps with dependencies, and it builds a graph: when you run tar_make(), it only recomputes what changed and whatever depends on it. It's a Makefile for R, but aware of your objects.

R · _targets.R
library(targets)
tar_option_set(packages = c("tidyverse", "tidymodels"))

list(
  tar_target(file,   here::here("data/raw/churn.csv"), format = "file"),
  tar_target(raw,    readr::read_csv(file)),
  tar_target(clean,  clean_data(raw)),
  tar_target(model,  train_model(clean)),
  tar_target(report, quarto::quarto_render("reports/report.qmd"))
)
R · console
tar_make()          # runs only what's out of date
tar_visnetwork()    # draws the dependency graph
tar_read(model)     # retrieves an already-computed object
• skip target raw
• skip target clean
• run target model
• run target report
• end pipeline [2.1 seconds]

You only changed the model: targets skipped ingestion and cleaning (still up to date) and rebuilt only the model and the report that depends on it. On large projects this saves hours.

Document and test what matters

Don't document or test everything —that's exhausting and pointless—, but do cover the critical parts: the transformation functions the result depends on. A testthat test is a contract: "this function, with this input, must produce this output". If tomorrow someone breaks it by accident, the test shouts about it.

R · tests/test-utils.R
test_that("clean_currency converts text to number", {
  expect_equal(clean_currency("$1,250"), 1250)
  expect_true(is.na(clean_currency("N/A")))
})

Best practice: one .Rproj per project, paths with here(), dependencies with renv, style with styler/lintr, untouchable raw data, and the workflow orchestrated with targets. These six habits turn loose scripts into a project others can trust and reuse.

Common mistakes: committing heavy data/raw/ to Git (use .gitignore), installing packages with install.packages() globally instead of via renv, leaving an rm(list = ls()) at the top of every script (a symptom of a fragile workflow; restart the R session clean, not just the memory), and saving .RData on exit (disable it in Global Options).

Guided exercise

You have a folder with a single analysis.R that starts with setwd("C:/Users/ana/thesis"), reads data.csv from the desktop, repeats the same block three times to clean currency columns, and writes over the original CSV. Reorganize it into a professional project: create the folder structure, fix the paths, remove the duplication, and protect the raw data. List the concrete steps.

View solution
  1. Create the project: in RStudio, New Project → New Directory, which generates thesis.Rproj and pins the root.
  2. Structure the folders: create data/raw/, data/processed/, R/, scripts/, output/. Move data.csv to data/raw/.
  3. Portable paths: replace setwd(...) + read.csv("data.csv") with readr::read_csv(here::here("data/raw/data.csv")).
  4. Apply DRY: extract the repeated block into a clean_currency() function in R/utils.R and call it with source().
  5. Protect the raw data: the cleaned result is written with write_csv(data, here("data/processed/clean_data.csv")); the CSV in raw/ is never overwritten.
  6. Reproducibility: renv::init() and renv::snapshot(); create a README.md and a .gitignore that ignores data/raw/.
  7. Style: styler::style_dir() and lintr::lint_dir() to leave it tidy.

Result: anyone clones it, runs renv::restore(), and runs the analysis without touching a single path.

Challenge

Turn one of your own projects (or the one from the exercise) into a pipeline with targets: define at least four tar_target() —ingestion, cleaning, analysis and report—, visualize the graph with tar_visnetwork(), and check that when you change only the analysis stage, tar_make() skips ingestion and cleaning. Document in the README how to reproduce everything with two commands: renv::restore() and targets::tar_make().

Go deeper: R for Data Science (2nd ed.) · renv: Project Environments · The targets R package · Tidyverse Style Guide.

Professional portfolio

Your portfolio proves what you can do. In data science, showing > telling.

By the end you'll know: which projects to include in a portfolio that convinces a recruiter, how to publish clean code on GitHub with a README that speaks for itself, how to build a portfolio site with Quarto Websites that tells the story of each project, and —most importantly— how to communicate the why behind your decisions and the impact of your work, not just the code.

The mental model: you are what you show

A résumé says "I know how to use tidymodels". A portfolio proves it with a repository anyone can clone and run. In an interview, a link to one of your projects is worth more than half an hour of theoretical answers, because it removes the doubt: the recruiter sees your code, your way of thinking, and your ability to finish something complete. The why is information asymmetry: the company can't see how you work until they hire you… unless your portfolio shows them in advance.

What to include

  • 2-4 complete end-to-end projects: problem, data, analysis, visualization and conclusion. Better a few polished ones than many half-done.
  • Clean code on GitHub with a clear README, project structure (the one from the previous module) and reproducible results.
  • A site or blog built with Quarto Websites that tells each project's story for those who can't read code.
  • A diversity of skills: ideally a solid EDA, a dashboard or Shiny app, a machine learning model, and an automated report. Each demonstrates a distinct competency.

A good example of a project that "shows" is the Observatorio GEIH, a published Shiny app that explores Colombia's Gran Encuesta Integrada de Hogares (Great Integrated Household Survey): it starts from official data, processes it, and offers interactive visualizations. A piece like that, live and with a public link, communicates more than any list of technologies.

The README: your project's cover page

Most recruiters won't run your code; they'll read the README and glance at a couple of charts. That's why the README is the most cost-effective piece of your project. A good README answers, in this order: what problem does it solve?, what data does it use?, what did you find? (with a result image), how do I reproduce it? and what stack did you use?.

markdown · README.md
# Customer churn prediction

A model that identifies customers with a high probability of canceling,
so the sales team can act before losing them.

## Result
AUC 0.86 on the test set. The model detects 78% of the actual
churners (recall), prioritizing not letting at-risk customers slip away.

![ROC curve](output/figures/roc.png)

## How to reproduce it
```r
renv::restore()
targets::tar_make()
```

## Stack
tidymodels · xgboost · vip · plumber · Shiny · Quarto

The portfolio site with Quarto Websites

Quarto generates a static website from .qmd documents and publishes it for free on GitHub Pages. It's the most direct way to have a professional portfolio without knowing web development.

bash · create and publish
quarto create-project portfolio --type website
quarto preview            # view locally while you edit
quarto publish gh-pages   # publish on GitHub Pages

The structure of a Quarto portfolio site is minimal and readable: one global config file and one page per section.

structure · Quarto site
portfolio/
├── _quarto.yml       # config: navbar, theme, title
├── index.qmd         # cover page: who you are, in one sentence
├── projects.qmd      # gallery with cards linking to each case
├── about.qmd         # your story and way of working
├── posts/
│   ├── churn/index.qmd     # case 1: ML model
│   ├── geih/index.qmd      # case 2: Shiny dashboard
│   └── sales/index.qmd     # case 3: automated report
└── styles.css        # your own styles
yaml · _quarto.yml
project:
  type: website

website:
  title: "Luis Durango · Data Science"
  navbar:
    left:
      - href: index.qmd
        text: Home
      - href: projects.qmd
        text: Projects
      - href: about.qmd
        text: About

format:
  html:
    theme: cosmo
    css: styles.css

Tell the why and the impact

The difference between a run-of-the-mill portfolio and a memorable one isn't the model; it's the narrative. In each project, explain what decision you made and why (why did you prioritize recall over precision? why did you drop that variable?), and always close with the impact in business terms, not technical ones.

  • Technical and forgettable: "I trained an XGBoost with AUC 0.86".
  • Impactful and memorable: "the model lets the sales team focus their calls on the 20% of customers who account for 78% of the churn".
  • Impactful and memorable: "I automated the monthly sales report; it went from 4 hours of manual work to 2 minutes of quarto render".

In short: 2-4 complete and diverse projects (EDA, dashboard, ML, automation), each with clean code on GitHub, a README that answers what/why/how, and a page on your Quarto site that tells the story. Always communicate the why behind your decisions and translate your results into business impact.

Common mistakes: uploading a giant notebook with no README or context; ten identical tutorial projects (all the same iris dataset); repositories that don't run because data or dependencies are missing; and describing only the technique without saying what problem you solved or for whom.

Guided exercise

Take your best analysis and prepare it for your portfolio: create a Quarto site with a page dedicated to that project. It must include the problem in one sentence, a key chart, the conclusion with its impact, and a link to the GitHub repository. Plan the concrete steps, from creating the project to publishing it.

View solution
  1. Create the site: quarto create-project portfolio --type website.
  2. Configure _quarto.yml with your name, a navbar (Home, Projects, About) and a theme (theme: cosmo).
  3. Project page: create posts/my-project/index.qmd with a header answering problem → data → finding → impact.
  4. Insert the key chart as an executable R chunk, or as an image exported to output/figures/.
  5. Close with impact: one business line ("I cut X from 4 hours to 2 minutes") and the link [View code on GitHub](https://github.com/your-username/project).
  6. Preview with quarto preview and tweak.
  7. Publish with quarto publish gh-pages and add the URL to your résumé and LinkedIn.
Challenge

Build a portfolio with three projects that demonstrate distinct competencies: an EDA with visualization, an interactive dashboard (Shiny, like the Observatorio GEIH) and an end-to-end ML model. Publish it as a Quarto site on GitHub Pages, with a reproducible README in each repository and an impact narrative on each page. Share the public link as if you were about to send it to a recruiter.

Go deeper: Quarto Websites · Publishing to GitHub Pages with Quarto · R for Data Science: Communicate.

Advanced Level Project
Home/Advanced Level/Project

★ Final project: End-to-end Data Science

Bring it all together: from raw data to a deployed model. This is the project you'll put in your portfolio.

By the end you'll know: how to walk through the seven stages of a data science project —ingestion, cleaning and EDA, feature engineering with recipes, modeling with tidymodels and cross-validation, evaluation with appropriate metrics and interpretability, deployment with plumber and Shiny, and documentation with Quarto, README and Git— interpreting at each step what you did and why, not just how.

1 Concept2 Example3 Practice4 Challenge5 Project

The problem

Predict which customers will leave a service (churn) and deploy the model as an API + dashboard. It's a classic case, highly valued in companies, because retaining a customer costs far less than acquiring a new one: if the model warns in time, the sales team can step in. The business objective isn't to "be right a lot" in general, but rather not to let those who really will leave slip away. That sentence will determine which metric we choose.

The 7 stages

  1. Ingestion: load data (CSV/SQL) with a reproducible project.
  2. Cleaning and EDA: tidyverse + visualization to understand the problem.
  3. Feature engineering: create predictive variables with recipes.
  4. Modeling: train and compare models with tidymodels + cross-validation.
  5. Evaluation: appropriate metrics (recall, AUC) and interpretability (vip, SHAP).
  6. Deployment: expose the model with plumber + a Shiny dashboard.
  7. Documentation: Quarto report and README, all versioned on GitHub.

Stage 1 · Ingestion

We load the raw data with relative paths and turn it into a tidy object. We fix the type of the target variable from the start: churn must be a factor so tidymodels treats it as classification.

R · 01_ingest.R
library(tidymodels)
library(here)

data <- readr::read_csv(here("data/raw/churn.csv")) |>
  mutate(churn = factor(churn, levels = c("No", "Yes")))

glimpse(data)
Rows: 7,043 Columns: 21
$ customer_id <chr> "7590-VHVEG", "5575-GNVDE"…
$ tenure <dbl> 1, 34, 2, 45, 2…
$ monthly_charge <dbl> 29.85, 56.95, 53.85…
$ churn <fct> No, No, Yes, No, Yes…

Interpretation: 7,043 customers and 21 variables. Before modeling anything, we already know the size of the problem and that the target variable has two levels, with "Yes" as the class of interest (the one we want to detect).

Stage 2 · Cleaning and EDA

The EDA answers two questions: is the data healthy? and what relationships can we sense? The first thing we look at in churn is the class balance, because it conditions the entire evaluation.

R · balance and exploration
data |> count(churn) |> mutate(pct = round(100 * n / sum(n), 1))

data |>
  ggplot(aes(tenure, fill = churn)) +
  geom_histogram(position = "fill", bins = 30) +
  labs(y = "Proportion", title = "Churn by tenure")
  churn     n   pct
1  No   5174  73.5
2  Yes  1869  26.5

Interpretation: only 26.5% churn. This is an imbalance: a dumb model that predicts "nobody churns" would be right 73.5% of the time. That's why accuracy would fool us, and the histogram suggests that new customers (low tenure) churn much more: a strong hint that tenure will be predictive.

Stage 3 · Feature engineering with recipes

A recipe defines the data transformations as declarative steps. The critical advantage: it's fitted only on the training data and then applied to the test data, avoiding information leakage (data leakage). First we split the data.

R · split + recipe
set.seed(42)
split <- initial_split(data, prop = 0.8, strata = churn)
train <- training(split)
folds <- vfold_cv(train, v = 5, strata = churn)

rec <- recipe(churn ~ ., data = train) |>
  step_rm(customer_id) |>                            # identifier, not predictive
  step_impute_median(all_numeric_predictors()) |>    # fills numeric NAs
  step_dummy(all_nominal_predictors()) |>            # categoricals to indicators
  step_normalize(all_numeric_predictors())           # mean 0, sd 1

Interpretation: we use strata = churn so that train and test preserve the same 73/27 proportion; that keeps the evaluation honest. Each step_ has a why: we drop the id (uninformative), impute gaps, convert text to numbers, and normalize so that scale-sensitive models compete on a level playing field.

Stage 4 · Modeling with tidymodels + cross-validation

We compare two models —a logistic regression (simple, interpretable) and a gradient boosting (powerful)— using 5-fold cross-validation. CV estimates real performance without touching the test set, which we reserve for the end.

R · two models, one workflow
metrics <- metric_set(roc_auc, recall, precision)

log_mod <- logistic_reg() |> set_engine("glm")
xgb_mod <- boost_tree(trees = 500, learn_rate = 0.05) |>
  set_engine("xgboost") |> set_mode("classification")

wf_log <- workflow() |> add_recipe(rec) |> add_model(log_mod)
wf_xgb <- workflow() |> add_recipe(rec) |> add_model(xgb_mod)

res_log <- fit_resamples(wf_log, folds, metrics = metrics)
res_xgb <- fit_resamples(wf_xgb, folds, metrics = metrics)

bind_rows(
  collect_metrics(res_log) |> mutate(model = "logistic"),
  collect_metrics(res_xgb) |> mutate(model = "xgboost")
)
 .metric   mean   model
 roc_auc  0.842  logistic
 recall   0.541  logistic
 roc_auc  0.861  xgboost
 recall   0.783  xgboost

Interpretation: XGBoost wins on AUC (0.861 vs 0.842) and, above all, on recall (0.783 vs 0.541). Since our business objective is not to let anyone who's going to churn slip away, recall rules: we choose XGBoost.

Stage 5 · Evaluation and interpretability

Now we finally use the test set, just once, with last_fit(). We look at the confusion matrix (what errors does it make?) and explain the model with vip so we don't hand over a black box.

R · final evaluation
final <- last_fit(wf_xgb, split, metrics = metrics)
collect_metrics(final)                     # performance on the test set

collect_predictions(final) |>
  conf_mat(truth = churn, estimate = .pred_class)

# Variable importance
library(vip)
extract_fit_parsnip(final) |> vip(num_features = 8)
         Truth
Prediction   No   Yes
     No   947   81
    Yes    88  293

Interpretation: of 374 customers who actually churned, the model caught 293 (recall ≈ 0.78). The 81 false negatives are the error that hurts us most: customers who will leave without the model warning us. The vip chart shows that tenure, contract type and monthly charge are the biggest drivers of churn; for per-customer explanations you can complement it with SHAP values (the fastshap package), which decompose each prediction into the contribution of each variable.

Stage 6 · Deployment with plumber + Shiny

A model in an .rds is useless to anyone until someone can query it. We save the trained workflow and expose it as a REST API with plumber; on top of it we build a Shiny dashboard for non-technical users.

R · plumber.R
library(plumber)
model <- readRDS("output/models/churn_xgb.rds")

#* Churn probability for a customer
#* @post /predict
function(req) {
  new <- as.data.frame(req$body)
  predict(model, new, type = "prob")$.pred_Yes
}
R · start the API
plumber::pr("plumber.R") |> plumber::pr_run(port = 8000)
# POST http://localhost:8000/predict → churn probability

Interpretation: the API receives a customer's data and returns their churn probability. The Shiny dashboard consumes that logic through a form (tenure, contract, charge…) and shows the probability with a traffic light, so a sales manager can use it without writing a line of R.

Stage 7 · Documentation with Quarto + README + Git

The project wraps up with a Quarto report that tells the story (problem, data, decisions, results), a reproducible README, and the full history versioned in Git. Ideally the whole workflow is orchestrated with targets, so that tar_make() rebuilds data, model and report in a single pass.

R · reproduce everything
renv::restore()          # exact dependencies
targets::tar_make()      # ingestion → model → report
quarto::quarto_render("reports/churn.qmd")
R · pipeline skeleton
library(tidymodels)

# Data (example: telco churn)
data <- read_csv("data/raw/churn.csv") |>
  mutate(churn = factor(churn))

set.seed(42)
split <- initial_split(data, prop = 0.8, strata = churn)
folds <- vfold_cv(training(split), v = 5, strata = churn)

rec <- recipe(churn ~ ., data = training(split)) |>
  step_rm(customer_id) |>
  step_impute_median(all_numeric_predictors()) |>
  step_dummy(all_nominal_predictors()) |>
  step_normalize(all_numeric_predictors())

model <- boost_tree(trees = 500, learn_rate = 0.05) |>
  set_engine("xgboost") |> set_mode("classification")

wf  <- workflow() |> add_recipe(rec) |> add_model(model)
res <- fit_resamples(wf, folds, metrics = metric_set(roc_auc, recall))
collect_metrics(res)

final <- last_fit(wf, split)
collect_metrics(final)         # performance on the test set

In short: an end-to-end ML project isn't a model, it's a workflow: reproducible ingestion → EDA that defines the right metric (recall in imbalanced churn) → leak-free recipes → honest comparison with CV → test-set evaluation with interpretability → deployment as API + Shiny → documentation in Quarto and Git. Interpreting each stage in business terms is what separates an exercise from a portfolio project.

Common mistakes: optimizing accuracy with imbalanced classes; fitting the recipe on all the data (leakage into the test set); looking at the test metric many times until it "comes out nice" (that's already overfitting to the test); and delivering the model without explaining which variables drive it.

Final challenge

Complete the project end to end with a real dataset (Telco Customer Churn from Kaggle or similar) and add it to your portfolio. Walk through the seven stages: reproducible ingestion with here and renv; EDA that justifies your metric; features with recipes free of leakage; at least two models compared by cross-validation, choosing the best by AUC and recall; test-set evaluation with a confusion matrix and variable importance (vip and, if you're up for it, SHAP with fastshap); deployment of a prediction via plumber and a Shiny dashboard where a user enters a customer's data and gets their churn probability with a traffic light; and complete documentation in a Quarto report and a README, with the whole workflow orchestrated by targets and versioned on GitHub. In the README, close with the business impact in one sentence.

You made it! You've traveled the road from knowing nothing about R to building a complete, interpretable and deployable Data Science project, with the best practices of a professional. Now iterate: each new project will make you better and faster. Publish, share, ask for feedback, and keep building. Welcome to the professional level.

Resources
Home/Resources

Resources to keep learning

Books, references and communities to take your R to the next level.

Free books and references

  • R for Data Science (2nd ed., Wickham, Çetinkaya-Rundel, Grolemund) — r4ds.hadley.nz. The starting point for the tidyverse.
  • Advanced R (Wickham) — adv-r.hadley.nz. The inner workings of the language.
  • R Packages (Wickham, Bryan) — package development.
  • Tidy Modeling with R — tmwr.org. Machine learning with tidymodels.
  • Forecasting: Principles and Practice (Hyndman) — otexts.com/fpp3. Time series.
  • Posit Cheatsheets — posit.co/resources/cheatsheets.

Community

Posit Community, Stack Overflow (tag r), R-bloggers, Posit's YouTube channel, and the #RStats community on social media. To practice: TidyTuesday (a new dataset every week).
